Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
6 min
The Ship is Like a House: Majlis and Delegation of Divorce (Tafwid)
Commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq explaining the analogy of a ship to a house in tafwid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a ship is legally equivalent to a house in delegated divorce regarding change of session, and apply the rules of tafwid to scenarios of timing, repetition, and disputes.

Core Idea
A ship's session changes with its movement by water and wind, so it is like a house, not a riding animal.

In Hanafi fiqh, the location where a delegation of divorce (tafwid) occurs affects the validity of subsequent exercises of that delegation. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies that a ship is legally equivalent to a house—not a riding animal—because the session changes with the ship's movement. Understanding this distinction is crucial for determining when a wife may exercise her delegated right to divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main analogy
Determine what the author compares a ship to and why. Identify the key difference between a ship and a riding animal.
The issue is whether the session (majlis) changes with movement.
2
Understand the scope of delegation with 'kullama shi'ti'
Analyze the ruling that 'kullama' (whenever) allows divorce in any session, but only one at a time. Note the exception regarding a second husband (Zufar's ikhtilaf).
Contrast particles like 'idha', 'kayfa', etc. that restrict to the session.
3
Distinguish between 'la asha'' and choosing the husband
Apply the distinction: saying 'I do not wish' cancels only that wish, while choosing the husband terminates the entire delegation.
This distinction resolves an apparent contradiction in the text.
4
Apply the rules to dispute scenarios
Work through the cases from Jami' al-Fusulayn: wife's word accepted when delegation admitted but timing disputed; slave's word not accepted because master denies the act.
The key is whether the cause (delegation) is admitted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والفلك كالبيت . ومتی» قوله : (والفلك (كالبيت أي والسفينة كبيت لا كدابة ولا فرق بينهما حقيقة لتبدل المجلس حقيقة. وافترقا بأن سير الدابة يضاف إلى راكبها والسفينة إلى الماء والريح. وفي جامع الفصولين: لو قال لها أمرك بيدك كلما شئت فلها أن تطلق نفسها كلما شاءت في ذلك المجلس أو في مجلس آخر، إلا أنها لا تطلق دفعة واحدة أكثر من واحدة وإنما لها في المجلس تفريق الثلاث، فلو شاءت في العدة وقع لا بعد زوج آخر خلافاً لزفر. و«إذا» كـ«كلما في عدم التقييد بالمجلس لكن لا يفيد أن التكرار و«كيف» و«إن» و«حيث» و«كم» و«أين» و«أينما تتقيد بالمجلس والعتق كالطلاق في هذه المسائل حتى لو قال فيما لا يفيد التكرار لا أشاء ثم شاء العتق عتق وكذا الطلاق واستشكله مؤلفه بأنه مخالف لقولهم لو اختارت زوجها بطل وأجيب عنه فيما كتبته على جامع الفصولين بأنه يفرق بين اختيارها الزوج وبين قولها لا أشاء في مشيئة مكررة بأن الاختيار للزوج مبطل أصل التفويض وقولها لا أشاء إنما يبطل مشيئة من جملة المشيئات ولها المشيئة بعد ذلك فلا يبطل أصل التفويض . وفي جامع الفصولين أيضاً قال أمرها بيدها إن قامر ثم قامر وطلقت نفسها فقال إنك علمت منذ ثلاثة أيام ولم تطلقي في مجلس علمك قالت لا بل علمت الآن فالقول قولها. قال أمرك بيدك فطلقت نفسها فقال إنما طلقت نفسك بعد الاشتغال بكلام أو عمل، وقالت لا طلقت نفسي في ذلك المجلس بلا تبدله فالقول قولها لأنه وجد سببه بإقراره وهو التخيير، فالظاهر عدم الاشتغال بشيء آخر. وقال خيرتك أمس فلم تختاري وقالت قد اخترت فالقول قوله . قال لقنه جعلت أمرك بيدك . العتق أمس فلم تعتق نفسك وقال القن فعلته لا يصدق إذ المولى لم يقر بعتقه لأن جعل الأمر بیده لا يوجب العتق ما لم يعتق القن نفسه والقن يدعي ذلك والمولى ،ينكره ولا قول للقن في الحال لأنه يخبر بما لا يملك إنشاءه لخروج الأمر من يده بتبدل مجلسه. أقول : على هذا في مسألة الاشتغال بكلام إلى آخره ينبغي أن لا يقبل قولها .اهـ. وقد أجبت عنه في حاشيته بالفرق بينهما لأن في المسألة الأولى اتفقا على صدور الإيقاع منها بعد التفويض والزوج يدعي إبطال إيقاعها فلا يقبل منه، وفي الثانية لم يقر المولى بالإيقاع من العبد بعد التفويض .
And a ship is like a house. His statement (and a ship is like a house) means the ship is like a house, not like an animal, and there is no difference between them in reality because the session changes in reality. And they differ in that the movement of the animal is attributed to its rider, and the ship to water and wind. And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: If he said to her: 'Your affair is in your hand whenever you wish,' she may divorce herself whenever she wishes in that session or in another session. However, she may not divorce more than one at once, and she may separate the three in the session. So if she wishes during the waiting period, it occurs, not after another husband, contrary to Zufar. And 'idha' is like 'kullama' in not being restricted to the session, but it does not indicate repetition. And 'kayfa', 'in', 'haythu', 'kam', 'ayna', 'aynama' are restricted to the session. And manumission is like divorce in these matters. So if he said in something that does not indicate repetition: 'I do not wish,' then he wished manumission, it occurs, and similarly divorce. The author found this problematic because it contradicts their statement: if she chose her husband, it is invalid. And it was answered in my commentary on Jami' al-Fusulayn that there is a difference between her choosing the husband and her saying 'I do not wish' in repeated wishing: choosing the husband invalidates the original delegation, while her saying 'I do not wish' only invalidates one of the wishes, and she may wish afterwards, so it does not invalidate the original delegation. And in Jami' al-Fusulayn also: He said: 'Your affair is in your hand if you gamble,' then he gambled and she divorced herself. He said: 'You knew three days ago and did not divorce in the session of your knowledge.' She said: 'No, I only knew now.' The statement is her statement. He said: 'Your affair is in your hand,' so she divorced herself. He said: 'You only divorced yourself after engaging in speech or action.' She said: 'No, I divorced myself in that session without change.' The statement is her statement because its cause existed by his admission, which is the delegation. So the apparent is that there was no other engagement. And he said: 'I gave you choice yesterday, and you did not choose.' She said: 'I chose.' The statement is his statement. He said to a slave: 'I placed your affair in your hand for manumission yesterday, and you did not free yourself.' The slave said: 'I did it.' He is not believed because the master did not admit to his manumission, for placing the affair in his hand does not necessitate manumission until the slave himself frees himself, and the slave claims that and the master denies it. And there is no statement for the slave in this case because he is informing about what he cannot initiate, as the affair left his hand with the change of session. I say: Based on this, in the matter of engagement in speech etc., her statement should not be accepted. And I have answered in my gloss on it by the difference between them because in the first case they agreed on the occurrence of the divorce after delegation, and the husband claims to invalidate her divorce, so he is not believed. In the second, the master did not admit to the occurrence of divorce from the slave after delegation.
Source pages p. 565

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary (sharh) on the text of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, discussing the ruling on delegation of divorce (tafwid) when the husband says 'your affair is in your hand.' The author first clarifies the ruling that a ship is legally like a house, not like a riding animal, for the purposes of the session (majlis). The reason is that the session on a ship changes as the ship moves, similar to a house where the session is fixed to the place, whereas on an animal the rider's movement is attributed to him, so the session is not considered to change with the animal's motion. Then the text cites Jami' al-Fusulayn with various scenarios: if the husband says 'your affair is in your hand whenever you wish,' the wife may divorce herself at any time in the same or a different session, but she can only divorce one at a time (not three at once) and may separate the three divorces within the session. If she wishes during the 'iddah (waiting period) after the first divorce, it occurs, but not after marrying another husband, according to Abu Hanifa contrary to Zufar. The text then discusses various particles like 'idha' and 'kullama', explaining that some restrict to the session and some do not. The issue of the wife saying 'I do not wish' (la asha') is compared to choosing the husband; the latter invalidates the delegation outright, while the former only suspends that particular wish, so she may still divorce later. Several case studies from Jami' al-Fusulayn illustrate disputes about timing and change of session, with the general rule that where the husband admits to the delegation but disputes the timing or place, the wife's word is taken (qawl al-qawl) because the cause (the delegation) is established. However, in the case of a slave (qinn) who claims he freed himself by delegated authority, the slave's word is not taken because the master did not admit to the act of freeing, and the authority expires with the change of session. The commentator then notes an apparent contradiction in one case and resolves it by distinguishing between the wife's case (where the husband admits the divorce occurred but claims it was invalidated) and the slave's case (where the master denies any act of freeing).

Key Terms
مجلس (majlis): The meeting place where the delegation of divorce occurs; its change affects the validity of future exercise of the delegation.
تفويض (tafwid): Delegation of the right to divorce to the wife by the husband's statement.
مشيئة (mashi'a): The act of choosing to divorce by the wife in the context of delegation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following particles does NOT restrict the delegation of divorce to the current session?
A) 'idha'
B) 'kayfa'
C) 'kullama'
D) 'ayna'

Key Takeaway
A ship is legally like a house for the session in tafwid because its movement is not attributed to the rider; this affects when a wife may exercise delegated divorce.

The Delegated Divorce: Effect of 'Talliqi Nafsaki' and Valid Responses
Ruling on delegated divorce and valid responses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a husband says 'talliqi nafsaki' with or without intention, and identify which wife's response results in a valid divorce.

Core Idea
Delegated divorce takes effect according to the husband's intention; 'abantu nafsi' is valid, 'ikhtartu nafsi' is not.

This lesson examines the chapter on al-Mashi'a from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. You will learn the precise ruling when a husband delegates divorce to his wife and how her response must be phrased for it to take effect. Understanding this distinction prevents confusion between valid and invalid divorce pronouncements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the delegation scenario
The husband says 'talliqi nafsaki' without specifying a number. The ruling is that if the wife divorces, it counts as one revocable divorce unless the husband intended more.
2
Apply the intention effect
If husband intended three and she divorces three, all three occur; if he intended one but she divorces three, nothing occurs per Imam Abu Hanifa. Intention of generality is valid because 'talliqi nafsaki' means 'perform the act of divorce'.
3
Assess the wife's response phrases
'Abantu nafsi' (I have separated myself) is a valid response; 'Ikhtartu nafsi' (I chose myself) is not valid.
4
Check additional conditions
The delegation must be addressed specifically to the wife; general commands to 'whichever of my women' are ineffective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في المشيئة (ولو قال لها طلقي نفسك ولم ينو أو نوى واحدة فطلقت وقعت رجعية وإن طلقت ثلاثاً ونواه وقعن أي وقع الثلاث لأن قوله طلقي نفسك معناه افعلي فعل التطليق فهو مذكور لغة لأنه جزء معنى اللفظ فتصح نية العموم. وهو في حق الأمة ثنتان، وفي حق الحرة ثلاث، وقد تقدم الفرق بينه وبين قوله طلقتك» و«أنت طالق». وأشار إلى أن نية الثنتين لا تصح هنا أيضاً لكونه عدداً وأطلق تطليقها الثلاث فشمل ما إذا قالت طلقت نفسي ثلاثاً وقولها قد فعلت مع نية الثلاث كما في الخانية. وشمل ما إذا أوقعت الثلاث بلفظ واحد ومتفرقاً كما في فتح القدير. وقيد بنية الثلاث لأنها لو طلقت ثلاثاً وقد نوى واحدة لا يقع شيء عند الإمام كما سيأتي وقيد بخطابها لأنه لو قال طلقي أي نسائي شئت فطلقت نفسها أو قال أمر نسائي بيدك لم يقع شيء. كذا في الخانية، ثم اعلم أن المخاطب لم يدخل . تحت عموم خطابه، ودخل في قوله نسائي كلهن طوالق إذا دخلت الدار فإذا هي وغيرها. كما في الخانية أيضاً قوله : (وبأبنت نفسي طلقت لا باخترت) يعني إن أبنت نفسي يصلح جواباً لطلقي نفسك ولا يصلح اخترت نفسي جواباً هنا دخلت طلقت هي فصل في المشيئة قوله: (وقيد بخطابها لأنه الخ) فيه نظر فإن الخطاب موجود في مسألة الخانية أيضاً فكان عليه أن يقول قيد بقوله نفسك قوله : (يعني أن أبنت نفسي يصلح جواباً لطلقي هذا ظاهر في أنه لا يتوقف على إجازة الزوج لصدوره جواباً للأمر بالتطليق، وأما ما يأتي عن التلخيص فهو فيما إذا قالت أبنت نفسي ابتداء لا جواباً للأمر كما هنا، وإن أشكل عليك فارجع إلى ما كتبناه عن شرح
Chapter on Wishing (al-Mashi'a). And if he said to her: 'Divorce yourself' and he did not intend or intended one, and she divorced, it occurs as a revocable divorce. And if she divorced three and he intended three, they occur (i.e., the three occur) because his statement 'divorce yourself' means 'perform the act of divorce,' so it is mentioned linguistically because it is part of the meaning of the word, so the intention of generality is valid. And for a female slave, it is two, and for a free woman, three. And the difference between this and his statement 'I divorced you' and 'You are divorced' has preceded. And he indicated that the intention of two is not valid here either because it is a number, and he released her divorce of three, so it includes if she said: 'I divorced myself three times' and her statement: 'I have done it' with the intention of three, as in al-Khaniyyah. And it includes if she effected the three with one word or separately, as in Fath al-Qadir. And he restricted it with the intention of three because if she divorced three while he intended one, nothing occurs according to Imam Abu Hanifa, as will come. And he restricted it with addressing her because if he said: 'Divorce whichever of my women you wish' and she divorced herself, or he said: 'The affair of my women is in your hand,' nothing occurs. Likewise in al-Khaniyyah. Then know that the addressee does not enter under the generality of his address, but enters in his statement: 'All my women are divorced if the house enters' if she and others? As in al-Khaniyyah also. His statement: (And by 'I have separated myself' she is divorced, not by 'I choose') means that 'I have separated myself' is suitable as a response to 'Divorce yourself', and 'I chose myself' is not suitable as a response here. She entered, she divorced? Chapter on Wishing. His statement: (And he restricted with her address because... etc.) there is consideration in it, for the address is present in the case of al-Khaniyyah as well. So it was upon him to say: He restricted with his statement 'yourself'. His statement: (Meaning that 'I have separated myself' is suitable as a response to 'divorce') this is apparent that it does not depend on the husband's permission because it is a response to the order for divorce. And as for what comes from al-Talkhis, it is when she says 'I have separated myself' initially, not as a response to the order, as here. And if it is problematic for you, refer to what we wrote on the commentary.
Source pages p. 566

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the chapter on al-Mashi'a (the wish/act of choosing divorce) from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. It states the core ruling: if a husband says 'talliqi nafsaki' (divorce yourself) without any intention, or intending one divorce, and the wife then divorces herself, it counts as one revocable divorce. If she divorces three times and the husband intended three, all three occur. The reasoning is that 'talliqi nafsaki' is a command to perform the act of divorce; the word 'divorce' is part of the linguistic meaning. Therefore, the intention of generality (to effect three) is valid. The text distinguishes between a free woman (max three divorces) and a female slave (max two). It notes that the intention of two divorces is not valid here (similar to the husband's direct pronouncement). The ruling covers both cases where the wife says 'I divorced myself three times' or 'I have done it' with the intention of three. It also covers if she pronounces the three in one utterance or separately. However, if the husband intended only one but the wife divorces three, nothing occurs according to Imam Abu Hanifa (as will be explained later). Additionally, the text clarifies that the delegation must be addressed specifically to the wife; if the husband says 'divorce whichever of my women you wish' or 'the affair of my women is in your hand,' no divorce occurs because the delegation is not specified to her. This is from al-Khaniyyah. The text then discusses the response phrase: if the woman says 'abantu nafsi' (I have separated myself) in response to the husband's order, it is a valid divorce pronouncement; but saying 'ikhtartu nafsi' (I chose myself) is not valid as a response. There is a marginal note questioning the restriction to addressing the wife, pointing out that the case from al-Khaniyyah also involves an address. The commentator suggests that the proper restriction should be on the word 'nafsaki' (yourself) to indicate specificity. Finally, the mention of al-Talkhis distinguishes between a response to the husband's order and an initial pronouncement of separation.

Key Terms
مشيئة (mashi'a): The act of the wife exercising her delegated right to divorce; the name of this chapter.
أبنت نفسي (abantu nafsi): A phrase meaning 'I have separated myself,' used by the wife to effect a divorce in response to delegation.
اخترت نفسي (ikhtartu nafsi): A phrase meaning 'I chose myself,' which is not a valid response to delegation of divorce in this context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is not valid because she must say 'tallaqt'
B) It is valid and counts as one revocable divorce
C) It is valid only if she repeats it three times
D) It is invalid because it requires the husband's permission

Key Takeaway
When a husband delegates divorce using 'talliqi nafsaki', the wife's valid response 'abantu nafsi' effects divorce according to the husband's intention.

Distinction Between 'Abanti' and 'Ikhtari' as Responses to Delegated Divorce
Explaining the difference in legal effect between wife saying 'abantu nafsi' vs 'ikhtartu nafsi' when husband says 'talliqi nafsik'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for the wife's response 'abantu nafsi' vs 'ikhtartu nafsi' to delegated divorce and explain the proof from Sahaba consensus.

Core Idea
When a husband delegates divorce, only words of divorce work; 'ikhtartu' is not a word of divorce, as established by Sahaba consensus.

Delegated divorce (tafwid) raises tricky questions: when a husband says 'talliqi nafsik', which responses actually effect a divorce? The Hanafi school, relying on the consensus of the Companions, carefully distinguishes between words that are kinayah for divorce and those that are not. This lesson unpacks that distinction, clarifying why 'abantu nafsi' works but 'ikhtartu nafsi' does not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act of delegation
The husband says 'talliqi nafsik' or similar, granting the wife the power to divorce herself.
This is a tamlik (grant of ownership) of the right to divorce.
2
Classify the wife's response
Does she use a word that is explicit (sarih) or allusive (kinayah) for divorce, or a word like 'ikhtartu' that is not?
'Abantu' is a kinayah; 'ikhtartu' is not.
3
Apply the Hanafi rule
If she says 'abantu nafsi' with intention, a single revocable divorce occurs. If she says 'ikhtartu nafsi', no divorce occurs, regardless of intention.
The ruling for 'ikhtartu' is contrary to qiyas but upheld by ijma.
4
Note the analogy with emancipation
Similar ruling: if a master says 'i'tiqi nafsik' and the slave woman says 'ikhtartu', no emancipation occurs.
This confirms the principle that 'ikhtartu' is not an effective word for such rights.
5
Understand the proof structure
The proof is ijma of the Companions, which overrides the apparent analogy (qiyas).
This highlights the binding nature of Sahaba consensus in Hanafi usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والفرق بينهما أن الإبانة من ألفاظ الطلاق لأنه كناية والمفوض إليها الطلاق والاختيار ألفاظه لا صريحاً ولا كناية بدليل الوقوع بأبنتك دون اختاري وإن نوى الطلاق وتوقفه على إجازته إذا قالت أبنت نفسي بشرط نيتها كما في تلخيص الجامع، وعدم التوقف إذا قالت اخترت نفسي منه . وإنما صار كناية بإجماع الصحابة رضي الله فيما إذا حصل جواباً للتخيير على خلاف القياس، وصلح جواباً للأمر باليد أيضاً لأنه هو التخيير معنى فثبت جواباً له بدلالة نص إجماعهم على التخيير، لأن قوله أمرك بيدك ليس معناه إلا أنك مخيرة في أمرك الذي . الطلاق بين إيقاعه وعدمه فهو مرادف للتخيير بلفظ التخيير للعلم بأن اللفظ ملغى بخلاف طلقي فإنه وضع لطلب الطلاق لا للتخيير بينه وبين وفي المحيط من العتق : لو قال لأمته اعتقي نفسك فقالت اخترت كان باطلاً اهـ. بخلاف ما إذا قالت جعلت الخيار إلي أو جعلت أمري بيدي فإنه يتوقف فإذا أجاز صار أمرها بيدها كما قدمناه.
And the difference between them is that 'al-ibanah' (making bain) is from the words of divorce because it is a kinayah, and the one delegated to her divorce and choice are its words, neither explicit nor kinayah, as evidenced by occurrence with 'abantuk' (I made you bain) without 'ikhtari' (choose) even if he intended divorce, and its being conditional upon his acceptance if she said 'abantu nafsi' with the condition of her intention as in Talkhis al-Jami', and the lack of conditionality if she said 'ikhtartu nafsi' from him. It only became a kinayah by the consensus of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, when it occurs as a response to takhyir, contrary to analogy, and it was valid as a response to the command of 'amruki bi yadik' also because it is takhyir in meaning, so it is established as a response to it by the indication of the text of their consensus on takhyir, because his saying 'amruki bi yadik' only means that you are given choice in your affair which is divorce between its occurrence and its non-occurrence, so it is synonymous with takhyir by the word takhyir due to the knowledge that the word is disregarded, unlike 'talliqi' which is placed for requesting divorce, not for choice between it and [something else]. And in al-Muhit from emancipation: if he said to his slave woman 'i'tiqi nafsik' and she said 'ikhtartu', it is invalid. Unlike if she said 'ja'altu al-khiyar ilayya' or 'ja'altu amri bi yadi', then it is suspended, and if he accepts, her affair becomes in her hand as we have presented.
Source pages p. 567

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the commentator explaining the Hanafi position on delegated divorce. The core issue is the distinction between two possible responses a wife might give when her husband tells her 'talliqi nafsik' (divorce yourself). According to the Hanafi school, if she responds with 'abantu nafsi' (I have made myself bain), a single, revocable divorce occurs because 'ibanah' is one of the kinayah (allusive) words for divorce. The husband's delegation is fulfilled, though the wife added the description 'bain', which is disregarded. On the other hand, if she says 'ikhtartu nafsi' (I have chosen myself), no divorce occurs because 'choice' is not a word of divorce at all, neither explicit nor allusive. The proof for this is the consensus (ijma) of the Companions, which established that 'ikhtartu' does not effect divorce even when used as a response to a delegation, contrary to qiyas (analogy). The text also draws an analogy with emancipation ('itq): a master telling his slave woman 'i'tiqi nafsik' and her responding 'ikhtartu' is invalid. The commentator then notes that phrases like 'ja'altu al-khiyar ilayya' (I have made the choice to myself) or 'ja'altu amri bi yadi' (I have made my affair in my hand) require the husband's subsequent acceptance to take effect, unlike 'ikhtartu'.

Key Terms
إبانة (ibanah): A kinayah (allusive) word for divorce, meaning to make the wife bain (irrevocably separated).
اختيار (ikhtiyar): Choice; in this context, a statement of choosing oneself, which is not a word of divorce.
تفويض (tafwid): Delegation; the husband delegating the power of divorce to the wife by saying 'talliqi nafsik', 'amruki bi yadik', etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband tells his wife 'talliqi nafsik' and she responds 'ikhtartu nafsi'. According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling?
A) A single revocable divorce occurs.
B) No divorce occurs because 'ikhtartu' is not a word of divorce.
C) A bain divorce occurs because she expressed choice.
D) It depends on the husband's intention.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between 'abantu' and 'ikhtartu' in delegated divorce hinges on whether the word is a kinayah for divorce; 'abantu' works, 'ikhtartu' does not, based on Sahaba consensus.

Effect of Descriptive Words in Delegated Divorce
Ruling with proof via qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a wife adds a descriptive word in delegated divorce and explain why the description is ignored.

Core Idea
In delegated divorce, a wife's added description does not contradict the original act — only a change in number or substance nullifies the delegation.

The source passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a common scenario in delegated divorce: when a husband says 'talliqi nafsik' and the wife responds with a description like 'bain' or 'raj'i', does the divorce take effect? The passage establishes that only the original divorce occurs, and the description is surplus. It also contrasts the case where the husband specifies a number — then a violation would nullify the delegation entirely.

Guided Path
1
Identify the husband's delegation phrase
Determine what the husband said, e.g., 'talliqi nafsik' or an equivalent phrase that gives the wife authority to divorce herself.
The delegation must be clear and unconditional.
2
Analyze the wife's response
Examine whether the wife added a mere description (wasf) like 'ba'inatan' or 'raj'iyyatan', or changed the substance of the delegation (e.g., number of divorces).
The key distinction is between wasf (description) and asl (original act).
3
Apply the rule: violation in description is ignored; violation in the original nullifies
If the wife's response adds a description that does not contradict the fact of divorce, it is ignored. If she changes the number (e.g., husband said half, she said one), the violation is in the asl and no divorce occurs.
4
Recognize the analogy to emancipation ('itq)
The ruling is supported by qiyas to a master delegating emancipation: if the slave woman frees herself in the session, it is valid without added conditions. Similarly, in delegated divorce, the wife's action in the session suffices.
5
State the resulting ruling
When the wife adds a description, a single revocable (raj'i) divorce occurs; the description is void. When she changes the number, nothing occurs.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And he indicated by his saying 'tallaqat' that because her violation is only in the description, so the original divorce occurred without what she described it with, unlike if he said 'talliqi nisf tatliqa' and she divorced one, or 'thalatha' and she divorced a thousand, where nothing occurs because the violation is in the original. And in Fath al-Qadir: Know that the two issues were mentioned by al-Tamartashi and the disagreement in them in the original is only with regard to the form of the word, not otherwise, for if she executed in agreement, meaning the three and the half, what occurs would be what occurs with the one divorce and the thousand, and the disagreement in the issue of the book is with regard to meaning, for what occurs by mere explicit is not what occurs by the bain, and consideration is given to the mere word without violation in meaning, considering that it is the basis in effecting, and disagreement in meaning is not disagreement, and in it is what is not hidden. end. And there is no difference between his saying 'talliqi nafsik' and his saying 'talliqi nafsik tatliqatan raj'iyyatan', and no difference between her saying 'abantu nafsi' and her saying 'tallaqtu nafsi ba'inatan' in the occurrence of the original and the cancellation of the description, as in al-Bada'i'. And in it from emancipation: if he said to his slave woman 'amru 'itqiki fi yadik' or 'ja'altu 'itqiki fi yadik' or 'khayyartuki fi 'itqik' and she emancipated herself in the session, she is emancipated and does not need the master's intention, end. So it should be in divorce likewise, so these phrases become like 'talliqi nafsik'.
Source pages p. 567

Commentary
This passage is a ruling that establishes a key principle in Hanafi delegated divorce: when a husband delegates the power of divorce to his wife by saying 'talliqi nafsik', the wife's actual utterance of divorce will take effect as a single, revocable divorce (talq raj'i), even if she adds a description such as 'bain' (irrevocable) or 'raj'iyyah' (revocable) that does not match the original delegation. The description is considered surplus and is ignored. This is based on the idea that the wife has conformed to the original meaning (asl) of effecting a divorce; her addition is merely a description (wasf), not a contradiction of the essence. However, if the husband specifies a number (e.g., 'talliqi nusf tatliqa' or 'talliqi thalathan') and the wife deviates from that number (e.g., she says 'tallaqtu wahidah' when he said half, or 'tallaqtu alfa' when he said three), then no divorce occurs at all because the violation is in the original (asl) — the number is part of the substance of the delegated act, not just a description. The commentator then cites Fath al-Qadir to clarify that the disagreement between scholars is only about the wording, not the meaning. He also draws an analogy with emancipation ('itq): if a master says similar phrases to his slave woman (e.g., 'amru 'itqiki fi yadik') and she emancipates herself in the same session, she is free without needing the master's intention. By analogy, in divorce, such phrases are equivalent to 'talliqi nafsik' and the wife's action is effective.

Key Terms
وصف (wasf): A description added to the divorce utterance, such as 'bain' or 'raj'i'. It is considered surplus and does not affect the original divorce.
أصل (asl): The original act of divorce itself, as opposed to the description or number. Violation in the asl invalidates the entire delegation.
مخالفة (mukhalafah): Violation or deviation by the wife from what the husband intended in the delegation. If in the description, it is ignored; if in the asl, it nullifies.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says to his wife 'talliqi nafsik' and she responds 'tallaqtu nafsi tatliqatan bainatan'. What is the result according to the Hanafi school?
A) A bain divorce occurs.
B) A raj'i divorce occurs because the description 'bain' is ignored.
C) No divorce occurs because she added a description.
D) It depends on her intention.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi delegated divorce, a wife's added description is ignored, and only the original divorce (raj'i) takes effect; a change in the number or substance nullifies the delegation.

Delegation of Divorce: Irrevocability and Valid Responses
Ruling on valid responses and irrevocability of delegation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Hanafi scholars deem the delegation of divorce (tamlik) irrevocable and list which verbal responses are valid for the wife.

Core Idea
Delegation of divorce is a tamlik, not a tawkil; it cannot be revoked and is restricted to the session.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq clarifies two critical aspects of delegated divorce: what the wife can say to effect the divorce, and why the husband cannot take back his delegation. The author combines fatwa literature from diverse Hanafi sources to establish the principles of tamlik versus tawkil.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two legal questions
The passage addresses: (1) Which words serve as valid responses from the wife when the husband says 'talliqi nafsik' or 'amruki bi yadik'? (2) Can the husband revoke the delegation after uttering it?
These are distinct but linked issues.
2
Distinguish tamlik from tawkil
Tamlik (grant of ownership) gives the wife the right to divorce herself; it is completed without her acceptance. Tawkil (agency) is when she acts for another, which is revocable and not session-bound. The delegation of divorce is tamlik, not tawkil.
The key is: she acts for herself, not for the husband.
3
Note the session restriction
The delegation is restricted to the majlis (session) by consensus, contrary to qiyas. She must exercise the right in the same session; otherwise it lapses.
This is an exceptional ruling.
4
Review the qiyas with debt discharge
The text draws an analogy from the creditor's statement 'ibr'i dhimmatak' (discharge your obligation). The debtor acts for himself essentially, similar to the wife in tamlik.
This supports the irrevocability and session-bound nature.

And we presented the lack of apparent difference between 'talliqi' and 'ibr'i dhimmatak' as everything that can be considered in one can be considered in the other. As for restricting to the session, it is by consensus contrary to analogy, end. And we presented in the chapter on choice that because the suspension of acceptance is not valid...
Source pages p. 568

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the commentary on delegated divorce, addressing two main points. First, it discusses which words can serve as valid responses when a husband says 'talliqi nafsik'. The text states that the wife does not need specific intention (niyyah) for certain phrases; rather, any word that can be used by the husband to effect divorce can be used by the wife as a response. For example, if the husband says 'amruki bi yadik' and the wife says 'ikhtartu', it is valid, but not if he said 'talliqi nafsik'. The commentary cites various fatwa collections: from al-Qunyah, that if she says 'halal Allahi 'alayya haram' (what Allah has made lawful upon me is forbidden), divorce occurs according to the practice in Khwarazm and Bukhara. From al-Bazzaziyyah, a table of suitable responses: 'ikhtartu' works for 'amruki bi yadik', 'tallaqtu' works for all. Then the text moves to the second major point: the husband cannot revoke the delegation (la yamlik al-ruju'). The delegation, whether uttered as takhyir, amr bi al-yad, or 'talliqi nafsik', is a tamlik (grant of ownership) that is completed by the wife alone without needing her acceptance (qabul). It has the meaning of ta'liq (suspension) in that it becomes effective upon the wife's action. Hence, restriction to the session (majlis) applies, but the husband cannot withdraw, dismiss, or prohibit her. The text distinguishes between tamlik and tawkil (agency): if a husband explicitly makes his wife an agent by saying 'wakaltuki fi talaqik', it is still considered tamlik, not tawkil, because she acts for herself, not for him. But if he delegates to her the divorce of another woman (e.g., his co-wife), that would be tawkil, and he can revoke it. The commentary from Fath al-Qadir draws an analogy with debt discharge: when a creditor says to a debtor 'ibr'i dhimmatak', it is similar to delegation. The underlying principle is that tamlik is established by the command alone without acceptance, as stated in al-Dhakhirah; this cannot be applied to regular agency (tawkil) where the agent acts for another, otherwise agencies would become irrevocable. The restriction to the session is an exceptional rule by consensus, contrary to qiyas.

Key Terms
تمليك (tamlik): A grant of ownership; in this context, the husband granting the wife the right to divorce herself. It is considered a form of transfer of authority, not agency.
توكيل (tawkil): Agency; when the husband appoints the wife as his agent to divorce herself, but Hanafi scholars treat it as tamlik if she acts for herself. True tawkil occurs when she acts for another (e.g., co-wife).
مجلس (majlis): The session in which the delegation occurs; the wife must exercise her right to divorce in the same session, otherwise the delegation lapses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says to his wife 'amruki bi yadik' in the morning. In the afternoon, before she acts, he changes his mind and says 'I revoke it.' Later, the wife says 'tallaqtu nafsi'. According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling?
A) The revocation is valid, so no divorce occurs.
B) The revocation is invalid; the divorce occurs because the delegation is irrevocable and she acted in the same session.
C) The revocation is valid only if she has not yet acted, but she must act in the same session.
D) It depends on the wording used.

Key Takeaway
The delegation of divorce is a tamlik that is irrevocable and session-bound; the wife's valid response effects divorce immediately.

Tamlik in Divorce: Irrevocability and Restriction to the Council
Commentary explaining the nature of tamlik in divorce delegation and its differences from agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on tamlik in divorce, explain why it is irrevocable and restricted to the council, and distinguish it from agency (tawkeel).

Core Idea
Tamlik in divorce is a transfer of ownership, not agency — the husband cannot revoke and the wife must act in the same council.

When a husband tells his wife 'divorce yourself' or 'your affair is in your hand', Hanafi fiqh treats this as a transfer of ownership of the right to divorce (tamlik), not as agency (tawkeel). This distinction leads to two critical consequences: the husband cannot revoke the delegation, and the wife must exercise her right in the same council unless the husband specifies otherwise. In this lesson, we will unpack the reasoning behind these rules and explore how they differ from agency and from other similar actions like discharging a debt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Focus on the husband's statement delegating divorce (e.g., 'divorce yourself'). The text rules it as tamlik, not tawkeel. The ruling is that the delegation is valid (sahih) and binding: the husband cannot revoke, and the wife's power is restricted to the council.
2
Locate the proof (dalil) for the restriction to the council
The passage explicitly cites 'consensus (ijma) contrary to analogy' as the proof. This means the rule is established by ijma, not by qiyas (analogy). Identify the phrase: 'وأما الاقتصار على المجلس فبالإجماع على خلاف القياس'.
This shows that the rule is a departure from pure analogy, justified by consensus.
3
Understand why the husband cannot revoke
The text argues that tamlik is completed by the husband alone without acceptance, and it contains a meaning of suspension (ta'liq). Therefore, the husband cannot revoke, remove her, or prohibit her. Trace the reasoning: tamlik → ownership → no need for acceptance → irrevocable.
Suspension here means that the divorce is conditioned on the wife's choice, but once delegated, the husband's control is extinguished.
4
Distinguish tamlik from tawkeel (agency)
Compare the features: tamlik is irrevocable and restricted to the council; tawkeel is revocable and not restricted. The text provides examples: if a husband delegates divorce of a co-wife or a stranger delegates his wife's divorce, it is tawkeel because the woman acts for another. Understand the basis: the agent works for another, the tamlik receiver works for herself.
The passage also mentions that explicit agency wording ('I appoint you as agent') is treated as tamlik for the wife's own divorce.
5
Examine the extended debate: tamlik vs ibra' (discharge)
Some scholars argued that 'divorce yourself' and 'discharge your liability' should be treated alike. The text explores two solutions: (1) divorce is detestable, so the husband intends the wife to act for herself; discharge is recommended, so he intends the debtor to act for him. (2) Divorce accepts conditionality, discharge does not. Accept both as valid Hanafi justifications.
This step is more advanced; focus on the main ruling first, then appreciate the scholarly depth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق فصل في المشيئة

ثلاثاً ونواه وقعن وبأبنت نفسي طلقت لا باخترت ولا يملك الرجوع وتقيد بمجلسها لا تحتاج إلى نية. وأفاد بعدم صلاحيته للجواب أن الأمر يخرج من يدها لاشتغالها بما لا يعنيها كما في فتح القدير ودل اقتصاره على نفي الاختيار أن كل لفظ يصلح للإيقاع من الزوج يصلح جواباً لطلقي نفسك كجواب الأمر باليد كما صرح به في الخلاصة. وذكر في القنية : قال لها طلقي نفسك فقالت حلال الله علي حرام يقع بخوارزم و بخارى اهـ. وفي البزازية اخترت يصلح جواباً لأمرك بيدك، ولاختاري لا لطلقي وطلقت جواباً للكل، والأمر يصلح تفسيراً للأمر لأن إقامة التعزير في الأول غير مفوض إليه، وكذا الاختيار للاختيار وطلقي نفسك يصلح تفسيراً لقوله أمرك بيدك ولقوله «اختاري» اهـ.

قوله: (ولا يملك الرجوع) أي ولا يملك الزوج الرجوع عن التفويض سواء كان بلفظ التخيير أو بالأمر باليد أو طلقي نفسك لما قدمنا أنه يتم بالمملك وحده من غير توقف على قبول وأنه تمليك فيه معنى التعليق، فباعتبار التمليك تقييد بالمجلس، وباعتبار التعليق لم يصح الرجوع عنه، ولا عزلها ولا نهيها . وفي جامع الفصولين والخانية : لو صرح بوكالتها فقال وكلتك في طلاقك كان تمليكاً كقوله طلقي نفسك .اهـ. بناء على أن الوكيل من يعمل لغيره وهذه عاملة لنفسها حتى لو فوض إليها طلاق ضرتها أو فوض أجنبي لها طلاق زوجته كان توكيلاً فملك الرجوع منه لكونها عاملة لغيرها ولا يتقصر على المجلس. وفي فتح القدير وكذا المديون في إبراء ذمته بقول الدائن له أبرئ ذمتك عامل لغيره بالذات ولنفسه ضمناً على ما قدمنا. والتوكيل استعانة فلو لزم ولم يملك الرجوع عاد على موضوعه بالنقض وقدمنا عدم ظهور الفرق بين طلقي وأبرئ ذمتك إذ كل ما يمكن اعتباره في أحدهما يمكن في الآخر، وأن عدم الرجوع أيضاً يتفرع على معنى الملك الثابت بالتمليك بناء على أنه يثبت بلا توقف على القبول شرعاً على ما صرح به في الذخيرة وأنه لا حاجة إلى ترتبه على معنى التعليق المستخرج لأنه يمكن مثله الوكالات والولايات، فلو صح لزم أن لا يصح الرجوع عن توكيل وولاية، وأما الاقتصار على المجلس فبالإجماع على خلاف القياس ا.هـ. وقد قدمنا في فصل الاختيار أنه لأنه لا يمكن مثله في الوكالات والولايات شرعاً لأنه لا . يصح تعليق الإجازة، بالزاي المعجمة

كتاب الطلاق فصل في المشيئة

079

بالشرط والطلاق يصح تعليقه وقد استمر على سهوه هذا ولو قال إنه يمكن مثله في التوكيل بالطلاق لكان صحيحاً لأن التعليق المستخرج يمكن فيه على معنى إن طلقتها فهي طالق مع أنه يصح الرجوع عنه وأما التوكيل بالبيع والولايات فلا دخل لها والله سبحانه وتعالى هو الموفق للصواب.

وقد ظهر لي الفرق بين طلقي) و أبرئ ذمتك وهو أنهما وإن اشتركا في العمل للنفس بتملكها نفسها وبراءة ذمته وللغير بامتثال أمر الزوج والدائن ولكن لما كان الطلاق محظوراً في الجملة وهو أبغض المباحات عند الله تعالى كما في الحديث، لم يكن مقصود الزوج إلا أن تكون عاملة لنفسها قصداً ولهذا قالوا: لا يكره التفويض وهي حائض. ولما كان الإبراء عن الدين مستحباً سبباً للثواب لم يكن مقصوده إلا أن يكون المديون عاملاً له لا لنفسه ليحصل الثواب له على فعل المستحب قصداً لا ضمناً.

ومن العجب ما ذكره الشارح الزيلعي في الوكالة عند قوله وبطل توكيله الكفيل بمال أن قول الدائن أبرئ ذمتك تمليك لا توكيل كما لو قال لها طلقي نفسك فإنه يلزم عليه تقييده بالمجلس وعدم صحة الرجوع عنه والمنقول خلافه. ومن العجب ما في معراج الدراية في فصل الاختيار أنه لا يلزم من كونه تمليكاً أن لا يصح الرجوع عنه لانتقاضه بالهبة فإنه تمليك ويصح الرجوع عنها فإنه على تقدير التسليم يلزم عليه التقييد بالمجلس، وقدمنا أنه لو أمره بإبراء نفسه لا يتقيد بالمجلس.

وذكر الفارسي في شرح التلخيص أن الفرق أن الطلاق والعتاق مما يقبل التعليق بالشرط فكان التفويض فيهما تمليكاً لا توكيلاً محضاً فاقتصر على المجلس والطلاق والعتاق مما يحلف به فكان يميناً فلم يمكن الرجوع عنه بخلاف التفويض في الإبراء وأخواته فإنها لا تقبل التعليق بالشرط فكان توكيلاً محضاً فلم تقتصر على المجلس وأمكن الرجوع عنه اهـ .

وفي الخانية من كتاب الوكالة : امرأة قالت لزوجها إذا جاء غد فاخلعني على ألف درهم كان ذلك توكيلاً حتى لو نهته عن ذلك صح نهيها، وكذلك إذا قال العبد لمولاه إذا جاء غد فأعتقني على ألف درهم .اهـ.

وفي كافي الحاكم: إذا وكل الرجل امرأته بخلع نفسها فخلعت نفسها منه بمال أو عرض فإن ذلك لا يجوز إلا أن يرضى، وهذا بمنزلة البيع في هذا الوجه. ولو قال لامرأته اشتر طلاقك مني بما شئت وقد وكلتك بذلك فقالت قد اشتريته بكذا كذا كان باطلاً، ولو قال لها اخلعي نفسك مني بكذا كذا ففعلت ذلك كان جائزاً ولا يشبه الطلاق بمال الذي يخلع بغير مال .اهـ.

وفي البزازية. من الخلع : اشتر فقالت اشتريت لا يقع ما لم يقل بعت، ولو قال اخلعي نفسك خلعت وقع بلا قبوله.

قوله : (وتقيد بمجلسها إلا إذا زاد متى شئت) لما قدمنا أنه تمليك

Book of Divorce, Chapter on Delegation. Three (divorces) if she intended them occur, and by 'I have made myself unlawful' she is divorced, not by 'I choose', and (the husband) does not have the right to revoke, and it is restricted to her council; she does not need intention. And it benefits by its unsuitability for response that the matter leaves her hand due to her preoccupation with what does not concern her, as in Fath al-Qadir. And his restriction to negating choice indicates that every word suitable for effecting divorce from the husband is suitable as a response to 'divorce yourself', like the response to the command with 'your hand is in your hand' as stated in al-Khulasa. And it is mentioned in al-Quniya: He said to her 'divorce yourself', she said 'What Allah has made lawful is forbidden to me' — it occurs (as divorce) in Khwarazm and Bukhara. End. And in al-Bazzaziyya: 'I choose' is suitable as a response to 'your affair is in your hand', but not to 'choose' for 'divorce me', and 'you are divorced' is a response to all. And the command is suitable as an interpretation of the command because the establishment of ta'zir in the first is not delegated to him, and similarly choice for choice, and 'divorce yourself' is suitable as an interpretation of his saying 'your affair is in your hand' and of his saying 'choose'. End. His statement: (and he does not have the right to revoke) i.e., the husband does not have the right to revoke the delegation, whether it is by the word of choice or by the command of the hand or 'divorce yourself', because we have stated that it is completed by the owner alone without waiting for acceptance, and that it is tamlik with the meaning of suspension. So by virtue of tamlik it is restricted to the council, and by virtue of suspension it is not valid to revoke it, nor to remove her, nor to prohibit her. And in Jami' al-Fusulayn and al-Khaniyya: If he explicitly made her an agent saying 'I appoint you as agent for your divorce', it is tamlik like his saying 'divorce yourself'. End. Based on the fact that the agent works for another while she works for herself. So if he delegated to her the divorce of her co-wife or a stranger delegated to her the divorce of his wife, it is agency, so he has the right to revoke from him because she works for another and it is not restricted to the council. And in Fath al-Qadir: similarly the debtor in discharging his liability by the creditor's saying to him 'discharge your liability' works for another essentially and for himself incidentally as we have stated. And agency is seeking assistance; if it becomes binding and he cannot revoke, it would contradict its purpose. And we have stated the lack of apparent difference between 'divorce yourself' and 'discharge your liability', for everything that can be considered in one can be considered in the other, and that the inability to revoke also branches from the meaning of ownership established by tamlik based on it being established without waiting for acceptance legally as stated in al-Dhakhira, and that there is no need to base it on the meaning of extracted suspension because its like can be found in agencies and governorships. If it were valid, then it would follow that revocation of agency and governorship would not be valid. As for restriction to the council, it is by consensus contrary to analogy. End. And we have stated in the chapter on choice that it is because its like cannot be found in agencies and governorships legally because it is not valid to suspend the authorization, with the letter zay. An error. He said in al-Fath: In the first it is apparent, and similarly in the second because the effecting of divorce by number when mentioned is not by description as previously, so it is a valid disagreement unlike our case because she disagreed in description after agreeing in the principle, so there is no disagreement since the description is subsidiary. His statement: (and the command is not suitable as an interpretation of the command) al-Bazzazi said: such as when he said 'your affair is in your hand' and she said 'my affair is in my hand'. And his statement because the establishment of ta'zir in the first is not delegated to him is not its place here; rather he mentioned it before this issue in the issues of beating without crime, and it seems it occurred in his copy in the margin so the author thought its place is here, or the mistake is from the copyist of his copy. His statement: (because it is not valid to suspend the authorization) i.e., which the agency entails, and the response of al-Nahr has passed. His statement: (and if) Book of Divorce, Chapter on Delegation. By condition, and divorce can be suspended, and he continued on this error. And if he had said that its like is possible in agency for divorce, it would be correct because the extracted suspension can be in it on the meaning of 'if you divorce her, she is divorced' yet he can revoke it. As for agency for sale and governorships, they have no relation to this. And Allah Almighty is the Granter of success. And the difference between 'divorce yourself' and 'discharge your liability' has become clear to me: although they share in working for herself by owning herself and discharging her liability, and for the other by obeying the command of the husband and creditor, but since divorce is forbidden in general and it is the most detestable of permissible things to Allah Almighty as in the hadith, the husband's intention is only that she work for herself directly. For this reason they said: it is not disliked to delegate when she is menstruating. And since discharge of debt is recommended and a cause for reward, his intention is only that the debtor work for him, not for himself, so that the reward for performing the recommended act accrues to him directly, not incidentally. And it is amazing what al-Zayla'i mentioned in Agency at his statement 'and his agency of the guarantor for property is invalid' that the creditor's statement 'discharge your liability' is tamlik not tawkeel, as if he said to her 'divorce yourself', but it would necessitate restriction to council and no valid revocation, yet the transmitted is contrary. And it is amazing what is in Mi'raj al-Diraya in the chapter on choice that it is not necessary that because it is tamlik, revocation is not valid, because it is refuted by gift: it is tamlik and revocation is valid. Because on acceptance of that, restriction to council would follow, and we have stated that if he ordered him to discharge himself, it is not restricted to council. And al-Farsi in Sharh al-Talkhis mentioned that the difference is that divorce and manumission accept suspension by condition, so delegation in them is tamlik not pure agency, so it is restricted to council. And divorce and manumission are things by which oaths are taken, so they become an oath, so revocation is not possible, unlike delegation in discharge and its likes, which do not accept suspension by condition, so they are pure agency, so they are not restricted to council and revocation is possible. End. And in al-Khaniyya from the Book of Agency: A woman said to her husband 'if tomorrow comes, divorce me by khul' for a thousand dirhams' — that is agency so that if she forbids him, her prohibition is valid. Similarly if a slave said to his master 'if tomorrow comes, emancipate me for a thousand dirhams'. End. And in Kafi al-Hakim: If a man appoints his wife as agent to divorce herself, and she divorces herself from him for property or goods, that is not permissible unless he consents. This is like a sale in this aspect. And if he said to his wife 'buy your divorce from me for whatever you wish and I have appointed you for that' and she said 'I have bought it for such and such' it is invalid. And if he said to her 'divorce yourself from me for such and such' and she did that, it is permissible, and it does not resemble the divorce for property where he divorces without property. End. And in al-Bazzaziyya from khul': 'buy' and she said 'I bought' does not occur unless he says 'I sold'. And if he said 'divorce yourself' and she divorced, it occurs without acceptance. His statement: (and it is restricted to her council unless she adds 'whenever you wish') because we have said it is tamlik. Yourself, from me, from me, she said. He said that it can (i.e., if the author of al-Fath said in his argument that there is no need to base it on the meaning of suspension that its like is possible in the case of appointing a stranger for divorce, for suspension is possible here yet he can revoke).
Source pages p. 568

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on the chapter of delegation (tamlik) in divorce. The core ruling is that when a husband delegates the power to divorce to his wife using phrases like 'divorce yourself' or 'your affair is in your hand', this is a tamlik (transfer of ownership), not a tawkeel (agency). Consequently, the husband cannot revoke the delegation, and the wife's exercise of the power is restricted to the council (majlis) in which the delegation was made, unless the husband adds 'whenever you wish'. The author explains that tamlik is completed by the husband alone without waiting for acceptance, and it contains the meaning of suspension (ta'liq), so it is irrevocable. This distinguishes it from agency, where the principal can revoke. The text then addresses a debate: some scholars argue that the distinction between tamlik and tawkeel is based on whether the action can be conditional. Divorce and manumission accept conditionality, so delegation in them is considered tamlik; actions like discharge of debt do not accept conditionality, so they are pure agency. Another view distinguishes based on the nature of the act: divorce is inherently detestable (abghad al-mubahat), so the husband intends the wife to act for herself, whereas discharge of debt is recommended, so the husband intends the debtor to act for him. The passage also discusses specific examples, such as when a wife says 'what Allah has made lawful is forbidden to me' in response to 'divorce yourself', which is effective as divorce in some localities. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that tamlik in divorce is indeed a transfer of ownership, not agency, with the consequences of irrevocability and restriction to the council.

Key Terms
تمليك (tamlik): A transfer of ownership of the right to divorce from the husband to the wife, making her the owner of the decision.
توكيل (tawkeel): Agency, where one person appoints another to act on his behalf; in divorce, it would be revocable and not restricted to the council.
مجلس (majlis): The council or session in which the delegation is given; the wife must exercise her right in that same session unless the husband specifies otherwise.
إبراء (ibra'): Discharge of a debt; the creditor's statement 'discharge your liability' is considered tawkeel, not tamlik, because it does not accept conditionality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, when a husband says 'divorce yourself' and the wife does not respond until the next day, what is the ruling?
A) The divorce is valid because the wife still has the right.
B) The divorce is not valid because the wife must act in the same council.
C) The divorce is valid if the husband intended it to be open-ended.
D) The divorce is not valid because the husband can revoke delegation.

Key Takeaway
Tamlik in divorce is a transfer of ownership: the husband cannot revoke, and the wife's decision is restricted to the council of delegation, a rule established by consensus despite being contrary to analogy.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
The Legal Distinction Between Taliqi and Ibri: Tamlik vs Tawkeel
Explains conceptual difference between delegating divorce and delegating discharge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between tamlik and tawkeel in delegation, and explain why taliqi is tamlik and ibri is tawkeel according to the Hanafi school, referencing the criterion of ta'liq.

Core Idea
Talaq accepts suspension (ta'liq), making delegation tamlik; ibra does not, making it tawkeel.

In Hanafi fiqh, delegating an action to another can be either a transfer of ownership (tamlik) or a mere agency (tawkeel), with different legal effects. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the distinction between delegating divorce (taliqi) and delegating discharge of debt (ibri). The key insight, provided by al-Farisi, is that divorce and manumission accept suspension by condition, making them tamlik, whereas discharge does not, making it tawkeel. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying rulings on assembly restriction and revocability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
The passage compares two commands: 'taliqi nafsik' (divorce yourself) and 'ibri dhimmata' (discharge your liability). Both are forms of delegation (tafwid).
Note that both involve the person acting both for themselves and for the command-giver, but the legal classification differs.
2
Assess the nature of each act
Divorce (talaq) is disliked but accepts suspension (ta'liq) and is used in oaths. Discharge (ibra) is recommended and does not accept suspension.
The acceptance of ta'liq is the decisive criterion.
3
Apply al-Farisi's rule
Because talaq accepts ta'liq, delegating it is tamlik (the wife owns the right to divorce). Because ibra does not accept ta'liq, delegating it is tawkeel (the debtor acts as agent).
This rule resolves the apparent contradiction raised by al-Zayla'i.
4
Derive the consequences
Tamlik in talaq is restricted to the assembly (majlis) and irrevocable. Tawkeel in ibra is not restricted to the assembly and revocable.
The passage notes that divorce is an oath, so revocation is impossible.
5
Test the distinction
Consider a scenario where someone says 'ibri dhimmata' – it is not limited to the assembly and can be revoked. For 'taliqi', it is limited and irrevocable.
This practical difference confirms the classification.

وقد ظهر لي الفرق بين طلقي) و أبرئ ذمتك وهو أنهما وإن اشتركا في العمل للنفس بتملكها نفسها وبراءة ذمته وللغير بامتثال أمر الزوج والدائن ولكن لما كان الطلاق محظوراً في الجملة وهو أبغض المباحات عند الله تعالى كما في الحديث، لم يكن مقصود الزوج إلا أن تكون عاملة لنفسها قصداً ولهذا قالوا: لا يكره التفويض وهي حائض. ولما كان الإبراء عن الدين مستحباً سبباً للثواب لم يكن مقصوده إلا أن يكون المديون عاملاً له لا لنفسه ليحصل الثواب له على فعل المستحب قصداً لا ضمناً. ومن العجب ما ذكره الشارح الزيلعي في الوكالة عند قوله وبطل توكيله الكفيل بمال أن قول الدائن أبرئ ذمتك تمليك لا توكيل كما لو قال لها طلقي نفسك فإنه يلزم عليه تقييده بالمجلس وعدم صحة الرجوع عنه والمنقول خلافه. ومن العجب ما في معراج الدراية في فصل الاختيار أنه لا يلزم من كونه تمليكاً أن لا يصح الرجوع عنه لانتقاضه بالهبة فإنه تمليك ويصح الرجوع عنها فإنه على تقدير التسليم يلزم عليه التقييد بالمجلس، وقدمنا أنه لو أمره بإبراء نفسه لا يتقيد بالمجلس. وذكر الفارسي في شرح التلخيص أن الفرق أن الطلاق والعتاق مما يقبل التعليق بالشرط فكان التفويض فيهما تمليكاً لا توكيلاً محضاً فاقتصر على المجلس والطلاق والعتاق مما يحلف به فكان يميناً فلم يمكن الرجوع عنه بخلاف التفويض في الإبراء وأخواته فإنها لا تقبل التعليق بالشرط فكان توكيلاً محضاً فلم تقتصر على المجلس وأمكن الرجوع عنه اهـ
And the difference between 'divorce yourself' and 'discharge your liability' has become clear to me: they are similar in that both involve action for herself (by owning herself and clearing his liability) and for the other (by obeying the husband's or creditor's command), but since divorce is generally prohibited and is the most hated of permissible things with Allah as in the hadith, the husband's intent is only that she act for herself intentionally; therefore they said: it is not disliked to delegate while she is menstruating. Since discharging debt is recommended and a cause for reward, his intent is only that the debtor act for him, not for himself, so that he may obtain the reward for doing the recommended act intentionally, not incidentally. It is amazing what al-Zayla'i mentioned in the chapter on agency regarding his statement 'and his agency as guarantor for property is invalid' that the creditor's saying 'discharge your liability' is ownership transfer (tamlik) not agency (tawkeel), just as if he said to her 'divorce yourself,' which would require its restriction to the assembly and the inability to revoke it, but the transmitted contrary. It is also amazing what is in Mi'raj al-Dirayah in the chapter on choice that it does not follow from it being ownership transfer that revocation is impossible, because it is invalidated by gift, which is ownership transfer and yet revocation is valid; for if that is granted, it would require restriction to the assembly, and we have already stated that if he orders him to discharge his own liability, it is not restricted to the assembly. al-Farisi mentioned in Sharh al-Talkhis that the difference is that divorce and manumission accept suspension (ta'liq) by condition, so delegation in them is pure ownership transfer, not agency, so it is restricted to the assembly; and divorce and manumission are used for oaths, so they are an oath and thus revocation is impossible, unlike delegation in discharge and its likes, which do not accept suspension by condition, so it is pure agency, so it is not restricted to the assembly and revocation is possible. End quote.
Source pages p. 569

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory ruling comparing two types of delegation: delegating divorce (tafwid al-talaq) and delegating discharge of debt (ibra'). The author first explains the underlying difference in intention: divorce is disliked, so the husband intends the wife to act for her own benefit (to own herself), whereas discharge is recommended, so the creditor intends the debtor to act for the creditor's benefit (to gain reward). Then several scholarly comments are cited. al-Zayla'i reportedly argued that 'discharge your liability' is tamlik, like 'divorce yourself,' which would imply restriction to the assembly and no revocation, but the transmitted ruling is contrary. The author of Mi'raj al-Dirayah refutes that using the analogy of gift (hiba), which is tamlik yet revocable. al-Farisi resolves the issue by noting that talaq and itq accept suspension, making them tamlik (and thus limited to assembly and irrevocable), while ibra does not accept suspension, making it pure agency (tawkeel) — not restricted to the assembly and revocable. This distinction is critical for understanding how 'taliqi' and 'ibri' function legally.

Key Terms
تمليك (tamlik): Transfer of ownership; here, the wife becomes owner of herself (i.e., gains the power to divorce herself immediately).
توكيل (tawkeel): Agency; the wife acts on behalf of the husband, not for herself.
تعليق بالشرط (ta'liq bil-shart): Suspension of a legal effect upon a future condition; talaq and itq accept this, ibra does not.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what distinguishes delegation of divorce (taliqi) from delegation of discharge (ibri)?
A) Taliqi is tamlik because talaq accepts ta'liq; ibri is tawkeel because ibra does not accept ta'liq.
B) Both are tawkeel, but taliqi is limited to the assembly.
C) Both are tamlik, but ibri is revocable.
D) There is no distinction; both are treated identically.

Key Takeaway
The acceptance of suspension (ta'liq) is the decisive factor that determines whether a delegation is tamlik (ownership transfer) or tawkeel (agency), with direct consequences for assembly restriction and revocability.

Khul' Delegation: 'Ishri' vs 'Ikhla'i'
Rulings on wife delegated to effect khul' with specific wordings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling distinguishing valid and invalid wordings when a husband delegates khul' to his wife, and identify when acceptance is required.

Core Idea
'Ikhla'i nafsaki' works without acceptance; 'ishri talaq' needs 'bi'tu' — wording determines validity.

Khul' (divorce for compensation) can be delegated to the wife, but the exact wording matters critically. This lesson examines several scenarios from the Hanafi texts: a conditional future request (pure agency, revocable), a general authorization (like a sale requiring agreement), and the specific phrase 'ikhla'i nafsaki' (direct delegation, no acceptance needed). The key distinction is whether the husband's words create a binding offer (requiring acceptance) or a direct empowerment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon in each sub-scenario (conditional request, general authorization, specific wording).
There are three main scenarios in the passage.
2
Classify the legal nature of the delegation
Is it agency (tawkeel) or a sale-like contract? Conditional future request is pure agency; general authorization is like a sale; 'ikhla'i' is direct empowerment.
The text explicitly says conditional request is tawkeel, and general authorization is like a sale.
3
Apply the ruling to each wording
For 'ishri talaq' (buy your divorce), khul' does not occur unless the husband says 'bi'tu' (I sold). For 'ikhla'i nafsaki' (divorce yourself), khul' occurs without the husband's acceptance.
From al-Bazzaziyyah: 'ashra qalat ishtaraytu la yaqa' ma lam yaqul bi'tu.'
4
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if the husband uses 'ishri', the wife's action is invalid unless he explicitly accepts; if 'ikhla'i', her action is valid immediately.
This distinction matters for fatwa: a mufti must advise on proper wording.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الخانية من كتاب الوكالة : امرأة قالت لزوجها إذا جاء غد فاخلعني على ألف درهم كان ذلك توكيلاً حتى لو نهته عن ذلك صح نهيها، وكذلك إذا قال العبد لمولاه إذا جاء غد فأعتقني على ألف درهم .اهـ. وفي كافي الحاكم: إذا وكل الرجل امرأته بخلع نفسها فخلعت نفسها منه بمال أو عرض فإن ذلك لا يجوز إلا أن يرضى، وهذا بمنزلة البيع في هذا الوجه. ولو قال لامرأته اشتر طلاقك مني بما شئت وقد وكلتك بذلك فقالت قد اشتريته بكذا كذا كان باطلاً، ولو قال لها اخلعي نفسك مني بكذا كذا ففعلت ذلك كان جائزاً ولا يشبه الطلاق بمال الذي يخلع بغير مال .اهـ. وفي البزازية. من الخلع : اشتر فقالت اشتريت لا يقع ما لم يقل بعت، ولو قال اخلعي نفسك خلعت وقع بلا قبوله
In al-Khaniyah from the Book of Agency: A woman said to her husband, 'When tomorrow comes, divorce me (khala'ni) for a thousand dirhams,' that is an agency, so if she prohibits him from that, her prohibition is valid. Similarly, if a slave says to his master, 'When tomorrow comes, free me ('atiqni) for a thousand dirhams,' [it is an agency]. End quote. In Kafi al-Hakim: If a man authorizes his wife to divorce herself (khala' nafsaha) and she divorces herself from him for property or goods, that is not permissible unless he agrees, and this is like a sale in this respect. If he said to his wife, 'Buy your divorce from me for whatever you wish, and I have authorized you for that,' and she said, 'I have bought it for such and such,' that is invalid. If he said to her, 'Divorce yourself (ikhla'i nafsaki) from me for such and such,' and she did that, it is permissible, and it is not like divorce for property which is divorce without property. End quote. In al-Bazzaziyyah from khul': If he says 'Buy' and she says 'I bought,' it does not occur unless he says 'I sold.' If he said 'Divorce yourself (ikhla'i nafsaki),' and she divorced, it occurs without his acceptance.
Source pages p. 569

Commentary
This passage collects several rulings on how a wife may be delegated to effect a khul' (divorce for compensation) and the proper wording. From al-Khaniyah: if the wife proposes a conditional khul' in the future, it is considered an agency, so she can revoke it. From Kafi al-Hakim: if the husband authorizes khul' in general, it is not valid unless he eventually agrees, like a sale; but if he explicitly says 'ikhla'i nafsaki' (divorce yourself from me) with a specified compensation, it is valid automatically. The distinction is that 'ishri talaq' (buy your divorce) is a contract of sale requiring offer and acceptance, whereas 'ikhla'i nafsaki' is a direct delegation that works without formal acceptance. The text from al-Bazzaziyyah confirms: 'Buy' requires 'I sell' from him; 'ikhla'i nafsaki' effects divorce without his acceptance.

Key Terms
خلع (khala'a): To divorce for compensation (khul'), a type of divorce with payment from wife.
توكيل (tawkeel): Agency; conditional future khul' request is pure agency and revocable.
اشتر (ishtari): 'Buy' – a word that creates a sale-like contract; requires 'bi'tu' (I sold) to be valid.
اخلعي (ikhla'i): 'Divorce yourself' – a direct delegation that effects khul' without acceptance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband tells his wife: 'Ikhla'i nafsaki minni bi alf dirham.' She says 'Khala'tu nafsi.' What is the ruling?
A) Invalid because no acceptance from husband.
B) Valid and khul' occurs without acceptance.
C) Valid only if she pays immediately.
D) Invalid because the amount was not specified.

Key Takeaway
Wording determines validity: 'ikhla'i' works without acceptance; 'ishri' requires 'bi'tu'.

Restriction of Delegated Divorce to the Assembly
Main ruling: delegated divorce is limited to assembly unless time phrase indicates generality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi default rule that delegated divorce (tamlik) is restricted to the assembly and identify which time phrases remove that restriction.

Core Idea
A delegated divorce is limited to the assembly unless a general time word like 'mata' gives her indefinite right.

When a husband delegates divorce to his wife, he transfers ownership of the right to divorce. The Hanafi school holds that this transfer is analogous to other property transfers, which are restricted to the assembly of the contract. However, if the husband adds a phrase indicating general time, such as 'mata shi'ta' (whenever you wish), the restriction is lifted. This lesson unpacks the rule, its proof, and the nuances of time words that affect the ruling.

Guided Path
1
State the default rule
Recognize that delegated divorce (tamlik) is restricted to the assembly (majlis) by default, as it is a form of ownership transfer.
The basis: 'tamlik' is limited to the assembly like any sale or gift.
2
Identify the exception
If the husband adds a general time phrase like 'mata shi'ta' (whenever you wish), she may divorce after the assembly because the word is general for times.
Other general time words include 'kullama' and under certain conditions 'idha' and 'hin'.
3
Understand the linguistic proof
The word 'mata' is classified as a general time adverb ('am fī al-awqāt), removing the default restriction. This is established by linguistic analysis.
If he says 'at any time you wish', it is explicitly general.
4
Distinguish restricting vs. non-restricting words
Words like 'in' (if), 'kayfa' (how), 'haythu' (where), 'kam' (how many), 'ayna' (where), 'aynama' (wherever) are conditional or specific and still restrict to the assembly. 'Kullama' is like 'mata' but adds repetition up to three.
The key is whether the word indicates generality of time.
5
Analyze the internal dispute on 'idha'
Imam Abu Hanifa considered 'idha' (when) to be like 'in' (if), thus restrictive. But the relied-upon view is that 'idha' can act as a time adverb (zarf) and then does not restrict. Since the right is certain, doubt does not remove it.
The practical ruling: with 'idha shi'ta', she can also divorce after the assembly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وتقيد بمجلسها إلا إذا زاد متى شئت) لما قدمنا أنه تمليك وهو يقتصر على المجلس وإذا زاد متى شئت كان لها التطليق في المجلس وبعده لأن كلمة متى عامة في الأوقات فصار كما إذا قال في أي وقت شئت ومراده من متى» ما دل على عموم الوقت فدخل إذا) وأورد عليه أنه ينبغي أن يكون إذا عند الإمام كـ«إن» كما تقدم في «إذا لم أطلقك» فيتقيد بالمجلس، وقدمنا جوابه بإمكان أن تعمل شرطاً فيتقيد، وأن تعمل ظرفاً فلا تتقيد والأمر صار في يدها بيقين فلا يخرج بالشك ودخل حين قال في المحيط : ولو قال حين شئت فهو بمنزلة قوله إذا شئت لأن الحين عبارة عن الوقت اهـ. وقيد بما يدل على عموم الوقت احترازاً عن إن» و«كيف» و«حيث» و«كم» و«أين» و«أينما» فإنه يتقيد بالمجلس. و«كلما كـ؛ متى في عدم التقييد بالمجلس مع اختصاصها بإفادة التكرار إلى الثلاث على ما أسلفناه في فصل الأمر باليد والإرادة والرضا والمحبة كالمشيئة بخلاف ما إذا علقه بشيء آخر من أفعالها كالأكل فإنه لا يتقصر على المجلس في الجميع
His statement: 'And it is restricted to her assembly except if he adds 'mata shi'ta' (whenever you wish)' because we have stated that it is ownership transfer (tamlik) and it is limited to the assembly. If he adds 'mata shi'ta', she may divorce in the assembly and after it, because the word 'mata' is general for times, so it becomes as if he said 'at any time you wish'; his intent by 'mata' is what indicates generality of time, so 'idha' enters. An objection is raised that 'idha' should be like 'in' according to Imam Abu Hanifa, as earlier in 'idha lam utalliquki', so it would be restricted to the assembly. We have given the answer earlier: it can act as a conditional (shart) and be restricted, or as a time adverb (zarf) and not be restricted; and the matter is definitely in her hand, so it is not removed by doubt. And 'hin' is included: al-Muhit says: if he says 'hina shi'ta' (when you wish), it is like 'idha shi'ta' because 'hin' is an expression for time. End quote. He restricted to what indicates generality of time to exclude 'in', 'kayfa', 'haythu', 'kam', 'ayna', 'aynama' — these are restricted to the assembly. 'Kullama' is like 'mata' in not being restricted to the assembly, with the peculiarity that it indicates repetition up to thrice, as we have stated in the chapter on the command with the hand. Will, pleasure, and love are like desire (mashiyyah). Unlike if he suspends it on another act of hers like eating, then it is not limited to the assembly in any case.
Source pages p. 569

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof explaining the main rule: when a husband delegates divorce to his wife, she must exercise the option in the same assembly (majlis), unless he adds a phrase indicating general time like 'mata shi'ta' (whenever you wish). The reason is that delegation (tafwid) is tamlik (ownership transfer), which is restricted to the assembly by default. The word 'mata' is general, so she can act anytime. The author addresses an objection: why doesn't 'idha' (if/when) function like 'in' (if) and restrict to the assembly? The answer: 'idha' can be either a conditional particle (restricting) or a time adverb (not restricting); since she definitely has the right, doubt doesn't remove it. The word 'hin' (time) also removes restriction. Phrases that restrict: 'in', 'kayfa', 'haythu', 'kam', 'ayna', 'aynama'. 'Kullama' also removes restriction but with the extra effect of repetition up to three times. If the husband suspends the delegation on another act of hers like eating, it is not limited to the assembly at all (since it's conditional, not tamlik).

Key Terms
متى (mata): Whenever; a time word indicating generality, removing assembly restriction.
مجلس (majlis): The assembly/meeting where the delegation is made; default limit for tamlik.
إذا (idha): When; can act as conditional (restricting) or time adverb (not restricting).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a husband says 'Idha shi'ti fa anti taliq', can the wife divorce after she leaves the assembly?
A) Yes, because 'idha' is a general time adverb like 'mata'.
B) No, because 'idha' is a conditional particle that restricts to the assembly.
C) It depends on the husband's intention, but the default is that it does not restrict because the right is certain.
D) No, because all delegated divorces are restricted to the assembly.

Key Takeaway
Delegated divorce (tamlik) is restricted to the assembly by default, but adding a general time word like 'mata shi'ta' gives the wife indefinite time; ambiguous words like 'idha' are resolved in her favor due to certainty of right.
