Delegation of Divorce: Distinction Between Agent and Woman, and Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Conditional Divorce
Explaining the distinction between agent and woman in kinayah divorce, and presenting an intra-Hanafi dispute on conditional divorce wording.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the validity of kinayah divorce when performed by an agent versus a wife, and analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on conditional divorce wording deviance.

Core Idea
Agent’s authority is literal; wife’s authority is general—this shapes the rules for conditional divorce.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra’iq tackles two interrelated issues: (1) why an agent for a revocable divorce cannot use kinayah (allusive) wording, while a woman commanded to divorce herself can; (2) what happens when a wife instructed to divorce herself with a specific type (bāʾin or rajʿī) conditionally upon her wish uses the opposite wording. The Hanafi masters differ—Abu Hanifa insists on exact compliance, while Abu Yusuf sees the condition fulfilled by the act of divorce itself. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly advising on divorce delegations and conditional divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two actors and their authorities
An agent for divorce (wakīl) is bound by the specific instructions of the husband; he can only divorce using the exact wording given. A woman whom the husband authorises to divorce herself (tamlīk) owns the power of divorce and can use any wording—explicit or allusive—that indicates divorce.
The key difference: agent's authority is limited by the instruction; woman's authority is broader because she is effectively given the right to choose the wording.
2
Apply the kinayah rule
If the agent was instructed to give a revocable divorce (rajʿī) but uses the kinayah word 'abantaha' (I made her irrevocable), he has acted beyond his authority and the divorce does not occur. In contrast, if a woman instructed to give a revocable divorce uses the same kinayah wording, the divorce is valid because she can use any wording.
Kinayah requires intention; the agent cannot supply intention because he is merely executing a command.
3
Analyze the conditional cases
Two cases are presented: (a) Husband says 'Divorce yourself one irrevocable divorce (bāʾin) if you wish,' and she divorces herself one revocable divorce (rajʿī). (b) Husband says 'Divorce yourself one revocable divorce if you wish,' and she divorces herself one irrevocable divorce. In both, Abu Hanifa holds that nothing occurs because she did not fulfill the condition regarding the type of divorce. Abu Yusuf holds that one revocable divorce occurs because the condition was on the act of divorce itself, not the specific type.
The dispute centers on what the condition attaches to: the act or the type.
4
Compare Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf
Abu Hanifa: The wife was commanded to divorce with a specific type; she deviated, so she did not act according to what was delegated; hence no divorce. Abu Yusuf: The condition 'if you wish' is fulfilled by her choosing to divorce; the difference in type does not nullify the divorce, but rather the divorce that occurs is the revocable type because that is the default when unspecified.
Abu Yusuf's view is described as 'قياس قول أبي حنيفة' (analogy from Abu Hanifa's statement), indicating a methodical extension.
5
Extract the agency principles
The passage also discusses general agency rules: if the principal specifies a time or condition, the agent cannot deviate. Specification by mention does not negate the ruling for other cases, except in three specific sale scenarios (selling to a particular person, with a guarantor, with a pledge). This reinforces the strictness of agency instructions.
This part helps contextualize why the agent's use of kinayah is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في المشيئة فيحتاج إلى الفرق بين قول الوكيل بالطلاق الرجعي أبنتها وبين المأمورة بالرجعي إذا قالت أبنت نفسي، ولعل الفرق مبني على أن الوكيل بالطلاق لا يملك الإيقاع بلفظ الكناية لأنها متوقفة على نية وقد أمره بطلاق لا يتوقف على النية فكان مخالفاً في الأصل بخلاف المرأة فإنه ملكها الطلاق بكل لفظ يملك الإيقاع به صريحاً كان أو كناية، وهذا الفرق صحته موقوفة على وجود النقل على أن الوكيل لا يملك الإيقاع بالكناية والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم. وفي الخانية من الوكالة : قال لغيره طلق امرأتي بائناً للسنة وقال لآخر طلقها رجعياً للسنة فطلقاها في طهر واحد طلقت واحدة وللزوج الخيار في تعيين الواقع ا.هـ. مع أن الوكيل بالطلاق له أن يطلق بعد طلاق الموكل ما دامت في العدة ولكن المانع من وقوع طلاقيهما التقييد بالسنة فإن السنة واحدة. وقيدنا في التصوير الأمر من غير تعليق بمشيئتها لما في الخانية من باب التعاليق، قال لها طلقي نفسك واحدة بائنة إن شئت فطلقت نفسها واحدة رجعية لا يقع شيء في قول أبي يوسف وهو قياس قول أبي حنيفة. ولو قال لها طلقي نفسك واحدة أملك الرجعة إن شئت فطلقت نفسها واحدة بائنة تقع واحدة رجعية في قول أبي يوسف، ولا يقع شيء في قياس قول أبي حنيفة لأنها ما أتت بمشيئة ما فوض إليها ا هـ. إلا أن يقال: إنه مستفاد مما قبله وقدمنا في مسائل التوكيل قبله بالطلاق أنه لو وكله بالمنجز فعلق أو أضاف لا يقع، وكذا لو قال طلقها غداً فقال أنت طالق غداً لأنه وكله بالتنجيز في غد وقد أضافه . ولو قال له طلقها بين يدي الشهود أو بين يدي أبيها فطلقها واحدة وقع كما في الواقعات وغيرها كقوله بعه بشهود فباعه بغيرهم. وحاصله أن التخصيص بالذكر لا ينفي الحكم عما عداه إلا في ثلاث مسائل مذكورة في وكالة الصغرى بعه من فلان بعه بكفيل بعه برهن. وقع النهي لا يملك المخالفة كقوله لا تبعه إلا بشهود إلا في قوله لا تسلمه حتى تقبض الثمن فله المخالفة
Chapter on Conditional Divorce: There is a need for a distinction between the statement of an agent for revocable divorce 'Abantaha' (I made her irrevocable) and the woman commanded with revocable divorce when she says 'Abantu nafsi' (I made myself irrevocable). Perhaps the distinction is based on the fact that an agent for divorce does not have the power to effect divorce with a word of kinayah (allusion) because it depends on intention, and he was commanded with a divorce that does not depend on intention, so he is contradictory in origin. In contrast, the woman, because she owns divorce with every word by which she can effect it, whether explicit or allusive. The correctness of this distinction depends on the existence of a narration that the agent does not have the power to effect divorce with kinayah. And Allah Most High knows best. And in Al-Khaniyah from the chapter of agency: He said to another: 'Divorce my wife irrevocably according to the Sunnah' and said to another: 'Divorce her revocably according to the Sunnah,' and they both divorced her in one pure period, one divorce occurred and the husband has the choice to specify which one occurred (end quote). Despite the fact that the agent for divorce may divorce after the divorce of the principal as long as she is in the 'iddah, but the impediment to both divorces occurring is the restriction to the Sunnah, for the Sunnah is one. And we restricted in the description the command without conditioning on her will because of what is in Al-Khaniyah from the chapter of conditional matters: He said to her: 'Divorce yourself one irrevocable divorce if you wish,' and she divorced herself one revocable divorce, nothing occurs according to the statement of Abu Yusuf, and it is the analogy of Abu Hanifa's statement. And if he said: 'Divorce yourself one divorce and I own the right of return if you wish,' and she divorced herself one irrevocable divorce, one revocable divorce occurs according to Abu Yusuf, and nothing occurs according to the analogy of Abu Hanifa's statement because she did not act according to the condition of what was delegated to her (end quote). Unless it is said: It is derived from what preceded. And we have presented earlier in the issues of agency before divorce that if he delegated an immediate divorce and he suspended or added a condition, it does not occur. Similarly, if he said: 'Divorce her tomorrow,' and he said 'You are divorced tomorrow,' because he delegated an immediate divorce tomorrow and he added a condition. And if he said: 'Divorce her in the presence of witnesses or in the presence of her father,' and he divorced her one, it occurs, as in Al-Waqi'at and others, like his saying 'Sell it in the presence of witnesses' and he sold it without them. The summary is that specification by mention does not negate the ruling from other than it except in three issues mentioned in the Minor Agency: 'Sell it to so-and-so,' 'Sell it with a guarantor,' 'Sell it with a pledge.' The prohibition occurs; he does not have the power to violate it, like his saying 'Do not sell it except with witnesses,' except in his saying 'Do not hand it over until you receive the price,' then he may violate it.
Source pages p. 582

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the distinction between an agent appointed for a revocable divorce and a woman who is commanded to divorce herself. The key point is that an agent, when using a kinayah (allusive) word like 'Abantaha' (I made her irrevocable), is exceeding his authority because he was instructed to give a divorce that does not require intention (i.e., a revocable divorce with explicit wording). In contrast, a woman who owns the power of divorce can use any wording, explicit or allusive, to effect divorce. The author notes that this distinction relies on a narration that an agent cannot use kinayah. The passage then presents several cases from Al-Khaniyah and other sources: (1) Two agents, one instructed for irrevocable Sunnah divorce, the other for revocable Sunnah divorce, both divorce in the same pure period; only one divorce occurs, and the husband chooses which one. (2) A wife is told to divorce herself irrevocably if she wishes, but she does so revocably; according to Abu Yusuf and the analogy of Abu Hanifa, nothing occurs because she did not fulfill the condition (the word 'bāʾin' was intended but she used 'rajʿī'). (3) If the husband says 'Divorce yourself one revocable divorce if you wish' and she gives an irrevocable divorce, Abu Yusuf says one revocable divorce occurs (because the condition was satisfied in the choice, but the type is modified), while Abu Hanifa's analogy says nothing occurs. The passage also discusses principles of agency: if an agent is delegated to give an immediate divorce and he suspends it on a condition, the divorce does not occur. The general rule is that specification (e.g., 'in front of witnesses') does not make the act invalid if omitted, except in three specific cases (sale to a particular person, with a guarantor, or with a pledge).

Key Terms
الوكيل بالطلاق (al-wakīl bi al-ṭalāq): An agent appointed by the husband to divorce his wife on his behalf.
الكناية (al-kināyah): An allusive word that implies divorce but requires intention (e.g., 'Abantaha' – I made her irrevocable).
التقييد بالسنة (al-taqyīd bi al-sunnah): The restriction that divorce must be according to the Sunnah (i.e., one divorce in a pure period).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband tells his wife 'Divorce yourself one revocable divorce if you wish' and she says 'I divorce myself one irrevocable divorce,' what is the ruling according to Abu Yusuf?
A) No divorce occurs
B) One irrevocable divorce occurs
C) One revocable divorce occurs
D) One irrevocable and one revocable divorce occur

Key Takeaway
The distinction between agent and woman in kinayah divorce and the intra-Hanafi dispute on conditional divorce highlight the importance of interpreting conditions attached to delegated authority—whether they bind the act or the type.

Conditional Divorce with 'In Shi'ta': The Effect of the Wife's Response 'Shi'tu'
Intra-Hanafi dispute on conditional divorce and the linguistic distinction between mashī'a and irāda

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on conditional divorce when the wife says 'Shi'tu' and distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on the requirement of intention.

Core Idea
The word 'Shi'tu' in conditional divorce is treated as acceptance based on custom, even without the word 'divorce'.

If a husband says 'You are divorced if you wish,' what happens when the wife simply says 'I wish'? The Hanafi school looks at the customary meaning of the word 'mashī'a' to determine if divorce has occurred. But even within the school, there is a difference: does the wife need to intend divorce, or is the word enough? This lesson unpacks that debate and the linguistic principle behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the husband’s statement
The husband says 'anti ṭāliq in shi'ta' – this is a conditional divorce (ta'liq). The condition is the wife's wish.
2
Analyze the wife’s response
She says 'Shi'tu' (I wish). According to Hanafi usul, 'mashī'a' in common custom implies the existence of the thing willed, unlike 'irāda' (intention). So 'Shi'tu' is taken as acceptance of the condition.
This is based on istihsan and 'urf.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa holds that 'Shi'tu' causes divorce only if the wife intends divorce. Abu Yusuf says the word itself, in this context, is sufficient to effect divorce even without explicit intention.
The mufta bihi follows Abu Yusuf's view.
4
Contrast with 'Aradtu'
If she says 'Aradtu' (I intended), no divorce occurs because 'irāda' does not imply actualization. This shows the linguistic precision required.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Given the scenario, state: if she says 'Shi'tu' intending divorce, one revocable divorce occurs. If she says 'Aradtu', no divorce. The fatwa is that 'Shi'tu' alone suffices.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت شئت إن كان كذا المعدوم بطل وإن كان لشيء مضى طلقت أنت طالق متى قوله : (وهي واردة على الكتاب قال الرملي وقد يقال لا ترد لانصرافه إلى المنجز دون المعلق تأمل قوله : (فإن فيه (الوجود كذا في النسخ والظاهر أن فيه تحريفاً والأصل «فإن فيه الموجود» أي فإن الشيء في العرف هو الموجود والمشيئة مأخوذة منه فتنبئ عن الوجود وعبارة الفتح : فتوجيهه أن يعتبر العرف فيه يعني يكون العرف العام أن الشيء الموجود والمشيئة منه قوله: (وهو سهو الخ) قال
She said 'I wish if it is such a non-existent thing' becomes void, and if it is for something past, she is divorced. 'You are divorced when...' He said: (It is an objection to the book) Al-Ramli said: It may be said it is not an objection because it applies to the immediate, not the conditional. Then reflect. He said: (For in it is existence) Thus in the copies, and apparently there is a corruption in it, the original is 'for in it is the existent', meaning that the thing in custom is the existent, and 'mashī’a' is derived from it, so it indicates existence. And the expression of Al-Fath: Its interpretation is to consider custom in it, meaning that the general custom is that the thing is existent and 'mashī’a' is from it. He said: (It is an oversight etc.) He said.
Source pages p. 583

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the conditional divorce where the husband says 'You are divorced if you wish' (anti ṭāliq in shi'ta). The wife's response 'Shi'tu' (I wish) has legal implications. If she says 'Shi'tu in kāna kadhā' (I wish if such a thing is), and it refers to a non-existent thing, the condition is void and no divorce occurs. If it refers to a past event, she is considered to have wished for the divorce and it takes effect. The passage then engages with a commentary: Al-Ramli objects to a statement in the matn (the base text) that 'Shi'tu' indicates existence; he argues that this applies only when the divorce is immediate, not conditional. The author of Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq notes that in some copies the word 'al-wujūd' appears, but it should be 'al-mawjūd' (the existent). The discussion from Al-Fath clarifies that in common usage, 'mashī’a' (will) implies the existence of the thing willed, unlike 'irāda' (intention) which does not necessarily entail existence. This distinction has practical fiqh implications: if a wife says 'Shi'tu ṭalāqī' (I wish my divorce), it signifies that she actually wants the divorce to happen now (existence), whereas 'Aradtu ṭalāqī' (I intended my divorce) does not imply actual occurrence. Therefore, in the scenario of 'anti ṭāliq in shi'ta', if the wife says 'Shi'tu' intending to accept the conditional offer, the divorce takes effect immediately. But if she says 'Aradtu' (I intended), it does not cause divorce because intention alone without expression of existence is insufficient.

Key Terms
المشيئة (al-mashī’a): The will or wish that implies actual existence of the action in common custom.
الإرادة (al-irāda): Intention or desire that does not necessarily imply actualization.
العارض (al-‘āriḍ): A textual objection in a commentary (here, Al-Ramli's objection to the matn).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) No divorce occurs because she did not say 'I divorce myself'
B) One divorce occurs if she intended it
C) Three divorces occur
D) The husband has a choice to accept or reject

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, 'Shi'tu' in response to a conditional divorce offer is legally operative as acceptance, based on the customary meaning of mashī'a implying existence.

Distinguishing Valid Expressions of Wife's Will in Conditional Divorce
Commentary on scenarios of talaq mu'allaq bi al-mashi'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish which verbal expressions by the wife constitute a valid mashi'ah (will) in a conditional talaq and apply the rulings on timing and combined conditions.

Core Idea
Only 'shi'tu' (I willed) with the sense of creating the divorce suffices; love, satisfaction, or acceptance do not unless in a financial transaction.

In Hanafi jurisprudence, a husband may make the occurrence of divorce conditional upon the wife's will. However, not every expression of desire or agreement qualifies as the required 'mashi'ah'. This lesson from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq unpacks the linguistic and legal distinctions between will, love, satisfaction, and intention, and explains how timing, session, and combined conditions affect the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Key Phrases
Read the passage and find the two different phrases: 'shi'ti al-talaq' (I will divorce) vs 'shi'ti talaqik' (I will your divorce). Note how the difference in pronoun attachment changes the need for intention.
The Arabic text uses 'شيئي الطلاق' and 'شيئي طلاقك'.
2
Compare Synonyms for Will
Extract the synonyms (ahbabti, raditi, qabiltu) and understand why only 'shi'tu' suffices because it implies 'creating' the divorce. Note the exception for financial transaction (talaq 'ala mal).
The text uses 'المحبة' and 'الرضا' as separate concepts.
3
Analyze Timing Rules
Work through the scenarios: delayed will (future time), advanced will (immediate), and past event (immediate). Note Abu Yusuf's difference on timing for 'in shi'ti fa-anti taliq ghadan'.
The key is whether the will is assigned to a specific time.
4
Examine Combined Conditions
Understand the distinction between single condition (e.g., 'in shi'ti wa abi'ti' without repeating 'in') – never falls – and separate conditions (repeating 'in') – whichever occurs triggers divorce. Also 'or' (aw) makes either sufficient.
Pay attention to the use of 'إن' (in) repetition.
5
Apply the Ruling to New Cases
Use the principles from this lesson to evaluate a novel scenario, such as husband saying 'if you intend (iradah) divorce' and wife says 'I am satisfied (raditu)'. State the ruling and reason.
The passage does not give a direct ruling on iradah + raditu; you must analogize from the given definitions.

Book of Divorce, Chapter on the Will (Mashi'ah). And similar: if the condition occurs, the divorce falls even if he did not intend. Its completion is in Fath al-Qadir, and it is an oversight, because the suspension on intention in his statement 'I will divorce' is because he did not attribute the divorce to her, so it is possible he delegated divorce of someone else. As for 'I will your divorce', it falls without intention because it means 'bring your divorce'. Thus in al-Muhit. And it is mentioned in al-Mawaqif that intention according to our companions is a third attribute, different from knowledge and power, that necessitates the specification of one of the two possibilities to occur. end. And in al-Muhit: if he said to her 'you are divorced if you love' and she said 'I willed', it falls because it contains the meaning of love and more. And if he said 'if you will' and she said 'I loved', it does not fall because it does not contain the meaning of bringing into existence, so the will was not found. And if he said 'if you will, you are divorced' and she said 'yes' or 'I accept' or 'I am pleased', it does not fall because he tied the divorce to her will literally, and that is not will, so the condition was not found. The book did not mention the case if he said 'you are divorced if you accept' and she said 'I willed'. It is narrated from al-Faqih Abu Bakr al-Balkhi that divorce falls because she brought acceptance and more, so it is like the case tied to love and she said 'I willed'. And Hisham mentioned in his Nawadir: if he said 'you are divorced for a thousand [dirhams] if you will', it does not fall until she accepts, contrary to his statement 'I accept', because this is a transaction and a transaction is not complete except with acceptance. end. The summary is that acceptance does not suffice for will except in divorce for money. And I have not seen the ruling if he tied it to intention and she responded with love or vice versa, or to satisfaction. In Sharh al-Musayarah: satisfaction is not objecting to a thing due to wanting its occurrence; love is willing that which is not followed by liability and consequence; and intention is more general, so it is separate from them when it relates to that which is followed by consequence. end. And her will must be in her session in the case of tying to will, love, satisfaction, intention, and everything that is from meanings that no one else can know, as in al-Muhit. And the author did not mention the added will. The summary of al-Muhit is that if the will is delayed from the time, like 'you are divorced tomorrow if you will', then the will is only for tomorrow. And if he puts the will first, like 'if you will, you are divorced tomorrow', it is mentioned in al-Ziyadat that she has the will immediately. From Abu Yusuf that she has the will at tomorrow. So if he said 'if you marry so-and-so, she is divorced if she wills' and he married her, she has the will in the session of knowledge. And if he said 'you are divorced yesterday if you will', she has the will immediately. end. In al-Mi'raj: if he said to her 'if you will, you are divorced' then said to another 'your divorce is with the divorce of this one', and she (the first) willed, she is divorced, and he intends for the other, because it is possible he meant his wife with her in that each is owned by him, not togetherness in occurrence. Thus in al-Mi'raj. And in it: if he said to her 'specifically' and she is al-Ramli: it is not an oversight because intention is necessary in will as al-Zayla'i mentioned, because will, even though it indicates existence, yet intention is necessary because he may intend its existence as occurrence or its existence as ownership, since divorce does not fall by doubt. In his statement 'I will divorce', it is possible he means 'bring about ownership', so how can we rule on it? By the oversight from al-Muhit, and it is another statement. And it has been presented that from it is understood that if he said 'I will your divorce', it falls with intention. The outcome is that in the issue there are two narrations, so one should not rule an oversight on someone who speaks based on one of them. Consider his statement: 'And the author did not explicitly restrict to the session etc.' The place of this is after his statement 'and if it was for a past thing, she is divorced', since nothing falls by what was presented from the text, so there is no difference between what is in the session and outside it. Source pages p. 584

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, chapter on divorce, section on the wife's will (mashi'ah). The text opens by discussing whether intention is required when the husband says 'I will divorce' (shi'ti al-talaq) versus 'I will your divorce' (shi'ti talaqik). The author of al-Hidayah claimed an oversight in Fath al-Qadir, but al-Ramli defends it, stating that intention is needed in the first phrase because it could mean delegating divorce to someone else, whereas the second phrase means 'bring your divorce' and so falls without intention. The passage then compares synonyms: 'love' (ahbabti) vs 'will' (shi'ti). If he says 'if you love' and she says 'I will', it falls because will includes love. Conversely, if he says 'if you will' and she says 'I love', it does not fall because love lacks the sense of bringing into existence. Similarly, 'yes', 'I accept', 'I am satisfied' are not will. An exception: if he says 'you are divorced for a thousand dirhams if you will', acceptance suffices because it's a transaction. The commentary defines satisfaction (rida), love (mahabbah), and intention (iradah) from Sharh al-Musa'irah, emphasizing that iradah is broader. The ruling requires the wife's declaration to occur in the same session (majlis) as the conditional statement, because it is a meaning not externally verifiable. The timing of the will: if the condition is future (e.g., 'tomorrow'), the will is only at that time; if the will precedes the effective time, she can will immediately according to al-Ziyadat, but Abu Yusuf says at the future time. For a past event ('yesterday if you will'), she must will immediately. The text then addresses complex scenarios: multiple wives, will tied to another's will (shi'ti in shi'ti), and combined conditions of will and rejection (abi'ti). The key distinction: if the husband phrases them as a single condition (e.g., 'if you will and if you refuse'), divorce never falls because it's impossible to simultaneously will and refuse. But if he repeats 'if' and makes each a separate condition, then whichever occurs triggers divorce. If he uses 'or' (aw), either condition suffices. The passage ends with a dispute between two narrations regarding 'in shi'ti wa abi'ti' without repeating 'in': one says it never falls (as in al-Muhit), another says it falls if either occurs. The commentator advises not to accuse someone of oversight for following one of the two narrations.

Key Terms
المشيئة (al-mashi'ah): The wife's volition or will expressed verbally to bring about the divorce condition; it is an act of creating existence (ijad).
الإرادة (al-iradah): A third attribute of Allah according to the Maturidi school; in this context, it is the wife's intention, broader than mahabbah and rida, and may involve liability.
الرضا (al-rida): Satisfaction; not objecting to something due to wanting its occurrence, but it is not equivalent to will (mashi'ah) because it lacks the element of active creation.
المحبة (al-mahabbah): Love; a desire for something that does not involve liability or consequence. It is narrower than iradah.
الإباء (al-iba'): Refusal; an active expression of rejecting the divorce, treated similar to will in combined conditions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says 'I will your divorce (shi'ti talaqik)' to his wife. According the mufta bihi position in this passage, what is the ruling on requiring intention (niyyah)?
A) Intention is required for the divorce to fall.
B) Intention is not required; the phrase directly means 'bring your divorce' and the condition is fulfilled with the wife's will.
C) Intention is required only if the wife remains silent.
D) Intention is required but if missing, the divorce still falls after a year.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi conditional divorce, only the wife's explicit 'I will' (shi'tu) constitutes valid mashi'ah; other expressions like love, satisfaction, or acceptance generally do not suffice unless in a financial transaction.

6 min
Suspending divorce on another's conditional wish
Ruling on divorce when wife's conditional wish fails

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to STATE the ruling when a wife makes her wish conditional on another's wish, and EXPLAIN why divorce does not occur based on Hanafi principles.

Core Idea
A wife's wish for divorce must be unconditional; a conditional wish invalidates the trigger.

In Hanafi fiqh, suspended divorce (ta'liq al-talaq) often depends on a condition such as the wife's wish. But what if the wife adds her own condition, linking her wish to another person's will? This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies that the wife's wish must be unconditional and immediate. Understanding this nuance prevents misapplication of conditional divorce rules and preserves legal certainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original condition
The husband says: 'You are divorced if you wish and if so-and-so wishes.' This sets two conditions: the wife's unconditional wish and the other person's wish.
2
Examine the wife's response
The wife says: 'I wish if so-and-so wishes.' She has attached a further condition to her own wish, making it conditional (mu'allaqah).
Contrast with the required unconditional (mursalah) wish.
3
Analyze the legal effect of the conditional wish
Since the husband's suspension required an unconditional wish from the wife, her conditional wish does not fulfill that condition. It is considered invalid (batil) as a trigger.
Only part of the condition (the other person's wish) is found, but the wife's valid wish is missing.
4
Conclude the ruling
Because the wife's valid unconditional wish is absent, the condition is not fully met. Therefore, divorce does not occur.
Even if the other person wishes, it is insufficient without the wife's unconditional wish.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق فصل في المشيئة مع أخرجي إن شئت ينوي الطلاق فشاءت طلقت وإن لم تخرج وأشار بقوله «شئت إن شئت» إلى كل مشيئة معلقة بمشيئة غيرها ولو كان الطلاق معلقاً على مشيئة ذلك الغير أيضاً لما في المحيط : لو قال أنت طالق إن شئت وشاء فلان فقالت قد شئت إن شاء فلان وقال فلان شئت لا يقع لأنه علق الطلاق بمشيئة مرسلة منجزة منها أتت بمشيئة معلقة فبطلت وهي مشیئتها، وبمشيئة فلان وجد بعض الشرط فلا يقع به الطلاق ا هـ .
Book of Divorce, Chapter on Conditional Will: With 'If you wish' (the husband intends divorce) and she wishes, she is divorced; and if she does not go out. And he indicated by his statement 'you wish, if you wish' to every volition suspended on another's volition. And if divorce is suspended on that other's volition as well, as in al-Muhit: If he says, 'You are divorced if you wish and if so-and-so wishes,' and she says, 'I have wished, if so-and-so wishes,' and so-and-so says, 'I wish,' it does not occur because he suspended divorce on an unconditional volition from her, and she produced a conditional volition, so it is invalid. And as for so-and-so's volition, part of the condition is found, so divorce does not occur by it. End quote.
Source pages p. 585

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Sharh explaining the matn's phrase 'أخرجي إن شئت'. The author clarifies that when a husband says 'if you wish' intending divorce, it is a suspension of divorce on her wish. If she wishes, divorce occurs. Then he moves to a case involving another person. He cites al-Muhit: A man says to his wife, 'You are divorced if you wish and if so-and-so wishes.' The wife responds, 'I wish if so-and-so wishes,' and so-and-so says, 'I wish.' The ruling is that divorce does not occur. The reasoning: The original suspension was on an unconditional wish from the wife, but she rendered her wish conditional, so her wish is invalid. As for the other person's wish, only part of the condition is fulfilled, but the wife's valid wish is missing, so the condition is incomplete. Hence no divorce. This highlights the Hanafi principle that the wife's wish must be unconditional and immediate.

Key Terms
مشيئة (mashī'ah): Volition or wish, specifically the wife's or a third party's will that triggers a suspended divorce.
معلقة (mu'allaqah): Suspended or conditional, referring to a wish that is made dependent on another condition.
مرسلة (mursalah): Unconditional, not tied to any further condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says to his wife: 'You are divorced if you wish and if Zaid wishes.' The wife says: 'I wish if Zaid wishes,' and Zaid says: 'I wish.' What is the Hanafi ruling?
A) Divorce occurs immediately upon Zaid's wish.
B) Divorce occurs because both wishes are present.
C) Divorce does not occur because the wife's wish was conditional.
D) Divorce occurs only if the wife repeats her wish unconditionally.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a wife's conditional wish in a suspended divorce invalidates her part of the condition, so divorce does not occur even if the other condition is later fulfilled.

Suspending Divorce on Combined Conditions: Single vs Separate
Distinguishes between single and separate conditions in suspended divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between making wish and refusal a single condition and making them separate conditions, and state the resulting divorce ruling for each structure.

Core Idea
A single impossible condition never triggers divorce; separate conditions trigger divorce upon any fulfillment.

This lesson unpacks the nuanced rulings from al-Muhit regarding expressions like 'if you wish and refuse' versus 'if you wish and if you refuse'. Understanding whether the husband's words create one condition or two is crucial for determining if and when divorce occurs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the expression structure
Look for repetition of 'in' (إن) and the position of the apodosis (جواب). If 'in' is not repeated, the conditions are likely single; if repeated, they may be separate. Also note if the apodosis is presented after each condition or delayed.
The key is whether the words make coexistence possible or impossible.
2
Determine possibility of the condition
If the condition requires both parts to occur simultaneously, ask: can they coexist? For wish and refusal, they cannot both happen at once, so the condition is impossible.
Impossible conditions in suspended divorce never trigger divorce.
3
Apply the Hanafi rule for single vs separate
If the condition is single and impossible, no divorce ever. If separate, divorce occurs upon the first fulfillment of any condition. If apodosis is delayed after both, they merge into one condition requiring both; if impossible, no divorce.
Separate conditions are like separate 'if' clauses: whichever occurs first triggers divorce.
4
Distinguish the case of 'or' (أو)
When 'or' connects the two conditions without repeating 'in', it is like an alternative: divorce occurs on the occurrence of either one, similar to separate conditions.
The ruling for 'or' is consistent with separate conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يذكر المصنف رحمه الله ما إذا علقه بمشيئتها وعدم مشيئتها أو بمشيئتها وإبائها أو باحدهما. وحاصل ما في المحيط أنه إن جعل المشيئة وإلا باء شرطاً واحداً وكذا المشيئة وعدمها فإنها لا تطلق أبداً للتعذر كأنت طالق إن شئت وأبيت أو إن شئت ولم تشائي وإن كرر «إن» وقد الجزاء كأنت طالق إن شئت وإن لم تشائي فشاءت في مجلسها طلقت إن قامت من غير مشيئة تطلق أيضاً لأنه جعل كلاً منهما شرطاً على حدة كقوله أنت طالق إن دخلت الدار وإن لم تدخلي فأيهما وجد طلقت، وإن أخر الجزاء كإن شئت وأن لم تشائي فأنت طالق لا تطلق بهذا أبداً لأنه التأخير صارا كشرط واحد وتعذر اجتماعهما بخلاف ما إذا أمكن اجتماعهما فإنها لا تطلق حتى يوجدا نحو إن أكلت وإن شربت فأنت طالق، وإن كرر (إن وأحدهما المشيئة والآخر الإباء كانت طالق إن شئت وإن أبيت فإن شاءت وقع وإن أبت وقع وإن سكتت حتى قامت عن المجلس لا يقع لأن كلاً منهما شرط على حدة والإباء فعل كالمشيئة فأيهما وجد يقع، وإن انعدما لا يقع وكذا لو لم يكرر ((إن) وعطف بـ«أو» كأنت طالق إن شئت أو أبيت لأنه علق الطلاق بأحدهما. ولو قال إن شئت فأنت طالق وإن لم تشائي فأنت طالق طلقت للحال، ولو قال إن كنت تحبين الطلاق فأنت طالق وإن كنت تبغضين فأنت طالق لا تطلق . والفرق أنه يجوز أن لا تحب ولا تبغض فلم يتيقن بشرط وقوع الطلاق، فأما لا يجوز أن تشاء أو لا تشاء فيكون أحد الشرطين ثابتاً لا محالة فوقع. ولو قال أنت طالق إن أبيت أو كرهت طلاقك فقالت أبيت تطلق ولو قال إن لم تشائي طلاقك فأنت طالق ثم قالت لا أشاء لا تطلق لأن قوله «أبيت» صيغة لإيجاد الفعل وهو الإباء فقد علق بالإباء منها وقد وجد فوقع. فأما قوله إن لم تشائي صيغة للعدم لا للإيجاد فصار بمنزلة قوله «إن لم تدخلي الدار فأنت طالق وعدم المشيئة لا يتحقق بقولها إلا شاء لأن لها أن تشاء من بعد إنما يتحقق بالموت
And the author, may Allah have mercy on him, did not mention the case where he suspends it on her wish and her non-wish, or on her wish and her refusal, or on one of them. The gist of what is in al-Muhit is that if he makes the wish and refusal a single condition, and likewise the wish and its absence, she is never divorced due to impossibility – like 'you are divorced if you wish and refuse' or 'if you wish and you do not wish.' But if he repeats 'in' and presents the apodosis – like 'you are divorced if you wish and if you do not wish' – then if she wishes in her assembly, she is divorced; if she rises without wishing, she is also divorced, because he made each a separate condition, like his saying 'you are divorced if you enter the house and if you do not enter,' so whichever occurs, she is divorced. And if he delays the apodosis – like 'if you wish and if you do not wish, then you are divorced' – she is never divorced by this, because the delay made them one condition and their coexistence is impossible, unlike when their coexistence is possible, where she is not divorced until both occur, like 'if you eat and if you drink, then you are divorced.' And if he repeats 'in' and one is wish and the other refusal, she is divorced: 'if you wish and if you refuse' – if she wishes, it occurs; if she refuses, it occurs; if she remains silent until she leaves the assembly, it does not occur, because each is a separate condition, and refusal is an action like wish, so whichever occurs, it occurs; if neither occurs, it does not occur. Similarly, if he does not repeat 'in' and connects with 'or' – like 'you are divorced if you wish or refuse' – because he suspended divorce on one of them. And if he says: 'If you wish, you are divorced; and if you do not wish, you are divorced' – she is divorced immediately. And if he says: 'If you love divorce, you are divorced; and if you hate divorce, you are divorced' – she is not divorced. The difference is that it is possible that she neither loves nor hates, so the condition for divorce is not certain. As for the first, it is impossible that she neither wishes nor does not wish, so one of the conditions is inevitably fulfilled, so it occurs. And if he says: 'You are divorced if you refuse or dislike your divorce,' and she says 'I refuse,' she is divorced. And if he says: 'If you do not wish your divorce, then you are divorced,' then she says 'I do not wish,' she is not divorced, because his word 'refuse' is a form for creating the action, which is refusal, so he suspended on her refusal, and it occurred. As for his saying 'if you do not wish,' it is a form for non-existence, not for creation, so it is like his saying 'if you do not enter the house, you are divorced,' and the absence of wish is not realized by her saying 'I do not wish' because she may wish later; it is only realized by death.
Source pages p. 585

Commentary
This lengthy passage from the Sharh addresses the missing rulings in the matn regarding cases where divorce is suspended on her wish and her non-wish, or wish and refusal. The author synthesizes from al-Muhit and other sources. First, if wish and refusal are made a single condition (without repeating 'in'), it is impossible, so divorce never occurs. If 'in' is repeated and the apodosis is mentioned after each, they become separate conditions: divorce occurs immediately upon fulfillment of either. If the apodosis is delayed after both, they become one condition requiring both, which is impossible, so divorce never occurs. If 'in' is repeated with wish and refusal, each is separate, so divorce occurs on either wish or refusal. If neither occurs (she stays silent until leaving the assembly), divorce does not occur. Using 'or' without repeating 'in' also makes them alternatives: divorce occurs on one. The passage then distinguishes between expressions: 'If you wish, you are divorced; if you do not wish, you are divorced' – divorce occurs immediately because one must be true. But 'If you love divorce... if you hate divorce' – divorce does not occur because she might be indifferent. Finally, the difference between 'refuse' (a positive action) and 'do not wish' (absence) is highlighted: saying 'I refuse' creates the refusal, so divorce occurs; saying 'I do not wish' does not create the absence, and she can still wish later, so divorce does not occur until death.

Key Terms
إباء (ibā'): Refusal, a positive action of rejecting divorce.
عدم المشيئة (adam al-mashī'ah): Absence of wish, a state of non-volition.
مجلس (majlis): The sitting/assembly where the statement is made; the time limit for expressing wish or refusal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says: 'You are divorced if you wish and if you refuse,' repeating 'in' twice and presenting the apodosis after each. What is the ruling if the wife refuses?
A) She is divorced immediately.
B) She is divorced only if she both wishes and refuses.
C) She is never divorced because the condition is impossible.
D) She is divorced only if she wishes.

Key Takeaway
The structure of the condition (single vs separate) determines whether divorce can occur; impossible conditions never trigger divorce, while separate conditions allow divorce upon fulfillment of any.

The Difference Between Refusal and Non-Wish in Conditional Divorce
Distinguishes between refusal (positive action) and non-wish (absence) in conditional divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'refusal' (ibā') as a positive action and 'non-wish' (adam al-mashī'ah) as a state, and apply the correct ruling when each is used in a conditional divorce.

Core Idea
Refusal is an act that fulfills a condition; non-wish is a state that may never be realized by words alone.

This lesson focuses on the critical distinction between expressions of refusal (ibā') and expressions of non-wish. The Hanafi school treats 'refuse' as a performative verb that creates the condition, while 'do not wish' merely reports an absence that may change. Understanding this difference is essential for correctly judging whether divorce has occurred.

Guided Path
1
Identify the verb used in the condition
Examine whether the husband uses a verb that indicates a positive action (e.g., 'refuse' - أبيت) or a verb that indicates absence (e.g., 'do not wish' - لم تشائي). The distinction is based on the linguistic form: 'abā' is a fi'l (action) while 'lam tasha'i' is a negation of a wish.
The key is whether the verb is performative (it creates the act) or merely descriptive.
2
Determine if the wife's response creates the condition
If the condition uses 'refuse', the wife's saying 'I refuse' creates the refusal, fulfilling the condition. If the condition uses 'do not wish', her saying 'I do not wish' does not create the state of non-wish, because she can still change her mind.
Non-wish is only definitively established at death.
3
Apply the ruling based on the verb type
For 'refuse': divorce occurs upon her refusal (verbal or actual). For 'do not wish': divorce does not occur by her mere statement; it only occurs if she never wishes until death (or perhaps never wishes at all, but that is uncertain in life).
The ruling for 'do not wish' is analogous to 'if you do not enter the house' – non-action does not fulfill the condition until death.
4
Contrast with the 'love' vs 'hate' case
The passage also contrasts 'love' and 'hate' with 'wish' and 'non-wish'. The key difference is that a woman can be indifferent (neither love nor hate), so the conditions are not necessarily fulfilled. But she cannot be indifferent to wish/non-wish – she must either wish or not wish.
This shows that logical certainty is necessary for immediate divorce.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يذكر المصنف رحمه الله ما إذا علقه بمشيئتها وعدم مشيئتها أو بمشيئتها وإبائها أو باحدهما. وحاصل ما في المحيط أنه إن جعل المشيئة وإلا باء شرطاً واحداً وكذا المشيئة وعدمها فإنها لا تطلق أبداً للتعذر كأنت طالق إن شئت وأبيت أو إن شئت ولم تشائي وإن كرر «إن» وقد الجزاء كأنت طالق إن شئت وإن لم تشائي فشاءت في مجلسها طلقت إن قامت من غير مشيئة تطلق أيضاً لأنه جعل كلاً منهما شرطاً على حدة كقوله أنت طالق إن دخلت الدار وإن لم تدخلي فأيهما وجد طلقت، وإن أخر الجزاء كإن شئت وأن لم تشائي فأنت طالق لا تطلق بهذا أبداً لأنه التأخير صارا كشرط واحد وتعذر اجتماعهما بخلاف ما إذا أمكن اجتماعهما فإنها لا تطلق حتى يوجدا نحو إن أكلت وإن شربت فأنت طالق، وإن كرر (إن وأحدهما المشيئة والآخر الإباء كانت طالق إن شئت وإن أبيت فإن شاءت وقع وإن أبت وقع وإن سكتت حتى قامت عن المجلس لا يقع لأن كلاً منهما شرط على حدة والإباء فعل كالمشيئة فأيهما وجد يقع، وإن انعدما لا يقع وكذا لو لم يكرر ((إن) وعطف بـ«أو» كأنت طالق إن شئت أو أبيت لأنه علق الطلاق بأحدهما. ولو قال إن شئت فأنت طالق وإن لم تشائي فأنت طالق طلقت للحال، ولو قال إن كنت تحبين الطلاق فأنت طالق وإن كنت تبغضين فأنت طالق لا تطلق . والفرق أنه يجوز أن لا تحب ولا تبغض فلم يتيقن بشرط وقوع الطلاق، فأما لا يجوز أن تشاء أو لا تشاء فيكون أحد الشرطين ثابتاً لا محالة فوقع. ولو قال أنت طالق إن أبيت أو كرهت طلاقك فقالت أبيت تطلق ولو قال إن لم تشائي طلاقك فأنت طالق ثم قالت لا أشاء لا تطلق لأن قوله «أبيت» صيغة لإيجاد الفعل وهو الإباء فقد علق بالإباء منها وقد وجد فوقع. فأما قوله إن لم تشائي صيغة للعدم لا للإيجاد فصار بمنزلة قوله «إن لم تدخلي الدار فأنت طالق وعدم المشيئة لا يتحقق بقولها إلا شاء لأن لها أن تشاء من بعد إنما يتحقق بالموت

If 'in' is repeated and the apodosis is mentioned after each, they become separate conditions: divorce occurs immediately upon fulfillment of either. If the apodosis is delayed after both, they become one condition requiring both, which is impossible, so divorce never occurs. If 'in' is repeated with wish and refusal, each is separate, so divorce occurs on either wish or refusal. If neither occurs (she stays silent until leaving the assembly), divorce does not occur. Using 'or' without repeating 'in' also makes them alternatives: divorce occurs on one. The passage then distinguishes between expressions: 'If you wish, you are divorced; if you do not wish, you are divorced' – divorce occurs immediately because one must be true. But 'If you love divorce... if you hate divorce' – divorce does not occur because she might be indifferent. Finally, the difference between 'refuse' (a positive action) and 'do not wish' (absence) is highlighted: saying 'I refuse' creates the refusal, so divorce occurs; saying 'I do not wish' does not create the absence, and she can still wish later, so divorce does not occur until death.

Key Terms
إباء (ibā'): Refusal, a positive action of rejecting divorce.
عدم المشيئة (adam al-mashī'ah): Absence of wish, a state of non-volition.
مجلس (majlis): The sitting/assembly where the statement is made; the time limit for expressing wish or refusal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says: 'You are divorced if you refuse your divorce.' The wife says 'I refuse.' What is the ruling?
A) She is not divorced because she must also wish.
B) She is divorced because refusal is a positive action.
C) She is divorced only if she also does not wish.
D) She is not divorced because the condition is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Refusal is a performative act that fulfills a condition; non-wish is a non-action that is only definitively established at death.

Suspending Divorce on One's Own Wish vs. a Foreigner's Wish
Explains assembly restriction in conditional divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between suspension on the husband's own wish and suspension on a foreigner's wish, and apply the Hanafi ruling on assembly restriction.

Core Idea
The husband's own wish is a pure condition; the foreigner's wish is a delegation limited to the assembly.

In conditional divorce, the scope of the condition's fulfillment varies. When a husband suspends divorce on his own mental state (e.g., 'unless I wish'), it is not bound by the assembly; he can wish at any time. However, if he suspends it on a third party's wish, that third party must respond in the same session. This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains the legal reasoning behind this distinction using istihsan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage contrasts suspension on the husband's own wish (e.g., 'unless I see otherwise') with suspension on a foreigner's wish (e.g., 'if so-and-so wishes').
2
Understand the ruling for the foreigner
The foreigner's wish is restricted to his assembly of knowledge. He must respond in the same session; otherwise the condition lapses and divorce occurs immediately.
3
Understand the ruling for the husband
The husband's own wish is not restricted to the assembly; he can express his wish at any time, even years later.
4
Analyze the legal reasoning
Default qiyas would treat all conditions as unlimited, but for the foreigner it is considered tamlik (delegation) which requires immediacy. For the husband, tamlik is impossible because he already owns divorce, so it remains a pure condition.
This is an example of istihsan (juristic preference).
5
Apply to the 'I do not wish' scenario
When a foreigner says 'I do not wish' in the assembly, despair arises and divorce occurs. When the husband says it, no despair because he can still wish later in life.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشار بتعليق الطلاق بمشيئتها إلى صحة تعليق عدد الطلاق بمشيئتها أيضاً فلذا قال في الذخيرة : لو قال لها أنت طالق ثلاثاً إلا أن تشائي واحدة، فإن شاءت واحدة قبل أن تقوم من مجلسها لزمتها واحدة، وكذا لو قال إلا أن يشاء فلان واحدة وإن لمن یکن فلان حاضراً فله ذلك في مجلس علمه وكذا لو قال أنت طالق ثلاثاً إلا أن یری فلان غير ذلك تقيد بالمجلس، وكذا لو قال إن لم ير فلان غير ذلك، وكذا لو قال إن رأى فلان ذلك فإنه يتقيد بالمجلس ا هـ. ولم يذكر المصنف كأكثر المؤلفين ما لو علقه بمشيئة نفسه وذكره في الذخيرة فقال : لو قال أنت طالق ثلاثاً إلا أن أرى غير ذلك فهذا لا يقتصر على المجلس حتى لو قال بعد ما قام عن المجلس رأيت غير ذلك لا يقع الثلاث، وكذلك لو قال إلا أن أشاء أنا غير ذلك فهذا لا يقتصر على المجلس، ولو قال لامرأته أنت طالق إن شاء فلان أو إن أحب أو إن رضي أو إن هوى أو إن أراد فبلغ فلاناً فله مجلس علمه بخلاف ما لو قال إن شئت أنا أو إن أحببت أنا لا يقتصر على المجلس. والفرق أن قضية القياس في الأجنبي أن لا يقتصر على المجلس كسائر الشروط لكن تركنا القياس في الأجنبي لأنه تمليك معنى وجواب التمليك يقتصر على المجلس، وهذا المعنى لا يتأتى في حق الزوج لأن الزوج كان مالكاً للطلاق قبل هذا فلا يتأتى منه التمليك فبقي هذا الشرط في حق الزوج ملحقاً بسائر الشروط فلم يقتصر على المجلس في حق الزوج. وإذا قال إن شئت أنا فالزوج كيف يقول حتى يقع الطلاق؟ لم يذكر محمد هذه المسألة في شيء من الكتب. وقال مشايخنا : ينبغي أن يقول شئت الذي جعلته إلي ولا يشترط نية الطلاق عند قوله «شئت ولا يشترط أن يقول «شئت طلاقك» لأن الطلاق لا يقع بقوله «شئت وإنما يقع بالكلام السابق لأن الطلاق بالكلام السابق، معلق بمشيئة اعتبرت شرطاً محضاً، فعند قوله «شئت» يقع الطلاق بالكلام السابق . والحاصل أن تعليق الزوج طلاق المرأة بصفة من صفات قلب نفسه ليس بتفويض وتمليك بوجه من الوجوه، ولو قال لها أنت طالق إن لم يشأ فلان فقال فلان لا أشاء في المجلس طلقت، ولو قال ذلك لنفسه ثم قال لا أشاء لا تطلق والفرق أن بقول الأجنبي لا أشاء يقع اليأس عن شرط البر وهو مشيئة طلاقها في المجلس، وقد تبدل من حيث الحكم والاعتبار بقوله لا أشاء لاشتغاله بما لا يحتاج إليه في الإيقاع فإنه يكفيه في الإيقاع السكوت عن المشيئة حتى يقوم عن المجلس أما بقول الزوج لا أشاء لا يقع اليأس عما هو شرط البر لأن المجلس وإن تبدل من حيث الحكم إلا أن شرط البر في حق الزوج عدم المشيئة في العمر والعمر باقٍ فلهذا لا يقع الطلاق ا هـ .
And he indicated by suspending divorce on her wish the validity of suspending the number of divorces on her wish as well. Hence, al-Dhakhira said: If he says to her, 'You are divorced three times unless you wish one,' then if she wishes one before she rises from her assembly, one divorce becomes binding. Similarly, if he says, 'Unless so-and-so wishes one,' even if so-and-so is not present, he has that option in his knowledge assembly. Similarly, if he says, 'You are divorced three times unless so-and-so sees otherwise,' it is restricted to the assembly. Similarly, if he says, 'If so-and-so does not see otherwise,' and similarly, 'If so-and-so sees that,' it is restricted to the assembly. End quote. And the author, like most authors, did not mention the case where he suspends it on his own wish, but it is mentioned in al-Dhakhira: If he says, 'You are divorced three times unless I see otherwise,' this is not restricted to the assembly; even if he says after rising from the assembly, 'I saw otherwise,' the three do not occur. Similarly, if he says, 'Unless I myself wish otherwise,' this is not restricted to the assembly. And if he says to his wife, 'You are divorced if so-and-so wishes, or if he loves, or if he is pleased, or if he desires, or if he intends,' and so-and-so is informed, he has his assembly of knowledge. Contrary to if he says, 'If I wish' or 'If I love,' it is not restricted to the assembly. The difference is that analogy requires the foreigner to not be restricted to the assembly like other conditions, but we abandoned analogy regarding the foreigner because it is a transfer of ownership in meaning, and the response to such transfer is restricted to the assembly. This meaning does not apply in the husband's case because the husband owned divorce before this, so transfer of ownership cannot arise from him; thus this condition in the husband's case remains attached to other conditions, so it is not restricted to the assembly regarding the husband. And if he says, 'If I wish,' how should the husband speak for divorce to occur? Muhammad did not mention this issue in any book. Our scholars said: He should say, 'I wish that which I made subject to my wish,' and the intention of divorce is not required when he says 'I wish,' nor is it required to say 'I wish your divorce,' because divorce does not occur by his saying 'I wish'; rather it occurs by the previous speech. Because divorce is by the previous speech, suspended on wish considered as a mere condition; so when he says 'I wish,' divorce occurs by the previous speech. The conclusion is that the husband's suspension of his wife's divorce on a quality of his own heart is neither delegation nor transfer of ownership in any way. And if he says to her, 'You are divorced if so-and-so does not wish,' and so-and-so says 'I do not wish' in the assembly, she is divorced. But if he says that about himself, then says 'I do not wish,' she is not divorced. The difference is that by the foreigner's saying 'I do not wish,' despair arises regarding the condition of release, which is his wish for her divorce in the assembly, and it changes in terms of ruling and consideration by his saying 'I do not wish' due to his engaging in what is not needed for effectuation; silence about the wish until he leaves the assembly suffices for effectuation. As for the husband's saying 'I do not wish,' despair does not arise regarding what is the condition of release because the assembly, although it changes in terms of ruling, the condition of release for the husband is the absence of wish throughout life, and life remains. Hence, divorce does not occur. End quote.
Source pages p. 586

Commentary
This passage from page 586 discusses the scope of the assembly (majlis) when divorce is suspended on a wish. Citing al-Dhakhira, it first confirms the validity of suspending the number of divorces on the wife's wish, and the wish must be expressed in the assembly. For a foreigner's wish (e.g., 'if so-and-so wishes'), it is also restricted to his knowledge of the statement and his response in the assembly. However, when the husband suspends divorce on his own wish (e.g., 'unless I wish otherwise'), it is not restricted to the assembly; he can wish or see otherwise at any time. The reason given is analogical: normally, conditions are not limited to the assembly, but for a foreigner, it is treated as a form of delegation (tamlik) which requires an immediate response. But for the husband, he already owns the right to divorce, so it remains a pure condition, thus not time-bound. The passage also explains how the husband should express his wish: he simply says 'I wish' without needing to mention divorce, because the divorce is already suspended on the wish. Finally, it distinguishes between a foreigner saying 'I do not wish' (which triggers divorce because it shows no chance of wish in the assembly) and the husband saying 'I do not wish' (which does not trigger divorce because he could still wish later in life). The husband's condition requires absence of wish for life, so despair only occurs at death.

Key Terms
مجلس (majlis): The sitting or gathering where the declaration is made; the time frame for responding to a conditional divorce.
تمليك (tamlik): Transfer of ownership or delegation of authority, here giving the wife or a third party the power to effect divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says: 'You are divorced unless I see otherwise.' Later, after leaving the assembly, he says 'I saw otherwise.' What is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The three divorces are cancelled.
B) The three divorces occur immediately upon his statement.
C) The three divorces do not occur because he saw otherwise within the assembly.
D) The three divorces do not occur because he can see otherwise at any time, and his statement after the assembly is valid.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between tamlik and pure condition determines whether the condition is assembly-bound: the husband's own wish is a pure condition, while a foreigner's wish is treated as tamlik and thus limited to the assembly.

Divorce Conditioned on Wife's Will: The Correct Ruling and Distinctions
Ruling statement on conditional divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a husband conditions divorce on his wife's will, and distinguish between the different phrasings and their effects.

Core Idea
Divorce conditioned on the wife's will only falls when she actually wills or refuses, unless immediate intent is expressed.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq, the author clarifies the correct ruling for a specific scenario: when a husband says to his wife 'If you will, you are divorced' or similar. The key is to distinguish between different phrasings and understand that the divorce does not take effect until the condition is fulfilled, unless the husband intended immediate falling. This lesson will guide you through the three opinions and the preponderant view, as well as an important application regarding the number of divorces.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition and its effect
The husband's statement 'If you will, you are divorced' conditions the divorce on the wife's will. Understand that such a conditional divorce is valid but does not take effect immediately unless the husband intends it to fall at once.
Conditional divorces are a distinct category from unconditional pronouncements.
2
Understand the three opinions and the correct one
The author notes that there are three opinions on this matter and selects as correct that the divorce does not fall until the condition (will or refusal) actually occurs. This is the mufta bihi position.
The other two opinions are not detailed, but the tarjih is clear.
3
Distinguish between 'if you will' and 'if you do not will'
The passage emphasizes a crucial distinction: if the husband says 'if you will' or 'you refuse', the divorce falls when that occurs; but if he says 'if you do not will', the divorce does not fall even if she does not will. The latter is treated like tamlik (delegation) where the wife's choice is not a condition for falling.
This distinction is specific to Hanafi fiqh.
4
Apply the ruling to conditioning the number of divorces
The author cites al-Dhakhira: if the husband says 'you are divorced three times unless you will one', and she wills one in the council, only one divorce falls. This shows that conditioning the quantity of divorce is valid and follows the same principle.
The wife's will must be expressed in the same sitting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل المشيئة يوججد أنه لا يقع حتى يوجد المشيئة والإباء إلا أن يعني الوقوع في الحال. وذكر قبله أنها إن شاءت يقع وإن أبت يقع كما لو كرر إن فحاصله أن فيها ثلاثة أقوال والصواب أنه لا يقع حتى أو يفرق بين «إن شئت» و «إن لم تشائي حيث لا يقع، وبين «إن شئت» و «أبيت» حيث يقع إذا وجدا. وأشار بتعليق الطلاق بمشيئتها إلى صحة تعليق عدد الطلاق بمشيئتها أيضاً فلذا قال في الذخيرة : لو قال لها أنت طالق ثلاثاً إلا أن تشائي واحدة، فإن شاءت واحدة قبل أن تقوم من مجلسها لزمتها واحدة.
Chapter on the will: It exists that it does not fall until the will and refusal exist, unless he intends falling immediately. And it was mentioned before that if she wills, it falls, and if she refuses, it falls, as if he repeated 'if'. So the summary is that there are three opinions, and the correct view is that it does not fall until there is a distinction between 'if you will' and 'if you do not will' where it does not fall, and between 'if you will' and 'you refuse' where it falls when they occur. And he pointed by conditioning divorce on her will to the validity of conditioning the number of divorces on her will as well. So he said in al-Dhakhira: If he said to her 'you are divorced three times unless you will one', and she willed one before leaving the council, then one divorce becomes binding on her.
Source pages p. 586

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq regarding divorce conditioned on the wife's will. The author states that the correct opinion is that the divorce does not occur until the condition (the wife's will or refusal) is actually brought about, unless the husband explicitly intended the divorce to fall immediately. The passage distinguishes two scenarios: (1) if the husband says 'if you will' and 'if you do not will', in that case the divorce does not fall because the condition is not fulfilled; (2) if he says 'if you will' or 'you refuse', the divorce falls when either occurs. This is based on the principle that a condition must be fulfilled for the conditional to take effect. The author also notes that conditioning the number of divorces on the wife's will is valid, as illustrated by the example from al-Dhakhira where a husband says 'you are divorced three times unless you will one' and the wife wills one in the council, resulting in only one divorce.

Key Terms
المشيئة (al-mashi'ah): The will or choice of the wife upon which divorce is conditioned.
الإباء (al-iba'): Refusal of the wife; when the husband conditions on refusal, divorce falls upon actual refusal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) It never takes effect because the condition is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The primary rule for conditional divorce on the wife's will: the divorce occurs only upon the fulfillment of the condition (will or refusal), unless the husband explicitly intended immediate falling.

The Confinement of Conditional Divorce: Third Party vs. Husband's Own Will
Explains the rulings when divorce is conditioned on the will of a third party versus the husband's own will, with confinement to council.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the rulings when divorce is conditioned on a third party's will and when conditioned on the husband's own will, and apply the Hanafi rule of confinement to the council based on the concept of tamlik.

Core Idea
A conditional divorce on a third party's will is confined to one council; on the husband's own will it is not.

In Hanafi fiqh, the ruling on conditional divorce depends critically on whose will is the condition. When a husband conditions divorce on the will of an outsider (e.g., 'if so-and-so wills'), the condition is confined to the council in which the outsider learns of it. But if he conditions it on his own will (e.g., 'unless I will otherwise'), it is not confined and remains effective throughout his life. This distinction arises from the concept of tamlik (delegation) and the application of istihsan over qiyas. This lesson unpacks the ruling and its proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Distinguish between conditioning divorce on the will of a third party (e.g., 'if so-and-so wills') and conditioning on the husband's own will (e.g., 'unless I will otherwise').
2
State the ruling for third-party condition
The condition is confined to the council (majlis) in which the third party learns of it. If the third party wills it in that council, divorce falls; if not, it does not fall even if he wills later.
This is based on istihsan treating it as a form of tamlik.
3
State the ruling for husband's own condition
The condition is not confined to a council. It remains effective throughout the husband's life. He can will or not will at any time.
Here, tamlik does not apply because the husband is already the owner of divorce.
4
Understand the proof: qiyas vs istihsan
By pure qiyas, both conditions would be treated the same—not confined—like any other condition. But Hanafi scholars abandoned qiyas for the third-party case due to istihsan, viewing it as a meaningful delegation (tamlik) that requires a response in the same council.
The husband's case remains under qiyas because he cannot delegate to himself.
5
Apply the distinction to examples
Work through scenarios: 'unless so-and-so wills' (confined) vs 'unless I see otherwise' (not confined). Also consider the case of 'if so-and-so does not will' and the effect of saying 'I do not will' in the council.
For a third party, saying 'I do not will' in the council fulfills the condition of despair; for the husband, it does not because the condition extends over his lifetime.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

يقتصر على المجلس، وهذا المعنى لا يتأتى في حق الزوج لأن الزوج كان مالكاً للطلاق قبل هذا فلا يتأتى منه التمليك فبقي هذا الشرط في حق الزوج ملحقاً بسائر الشروط فلم يقتصر على المجلس في حق الزوج. وإذا قال لأن الطلاق لا يقع بقوله «شئت وإنما يقع بالكلام السابق لأن الطلاق بالكلام السابق، معلق بمشيئة اعتبرت شرطاً محضاً، فعند قوله «شئت» يقع الطلاق بالكلام السابق . والحاصل أن تعليق الزوج طلاق المرأة بصفة من صفات قلب نفسه ليس بتفويض وتمليك بوجه من الوجوه، ولو قال لها أنت طالق إن لم يشأ فلان فقال فلان لا أشاء في المجلس طلقت، ولو قال ذلك لنفسه ثم قال لا أشاء لا تطلق والفرق أن بقول الأجنبي لا أشاء يقع اليأس عن شرط البر وهو مشيئة طلاقها في المجلس، وقد تبدل من حيث الحكم والاعتبار بقوله لا أشاء لاشتغاله بما لا يحتاج إليه في الإيقاع فإنه يكفيه في الإيقاع السكوت عن المشيئة حتى يقوم عن المجلس أما بقول الزوج لا أشاء لا يقع اليأس عما هو شرط البر لأن المجلس وإن تبدل من حيث الحكم إلا أن شرط البر في حق الزوج عدم المشيئة في العمر والعمر باقٍ فلهذا لا يقع الطلاق ا هـ . وفي الجامع للصدر الشهيد: قال شئت أو متى أنت طالق إن شاء فلان أو أراد أو رضي أو هوى فيقتصر على مجلس علمه لأنه تمليك بخلاف إضافته إلى نفسه ولو قال إن لم يشأ أو إن لم يرد فقام من مجلسه أو قال فيه لا أشاء طلقت بخلاف إن لم يشأ اليوم، ولو قال إن لم أشأ إن لم أرد فقام أو قال لا أشاء لا تطلق قبل موته بخلاف إن أبيت طلاقك أو كرهت ا هـ . وفي الخانية : أنت طالق ثلاثاً وفلانة واحدة إن شئت فشاءت واحدة لفلانة طلقت فلانة واحدة ويبطل عنها الثلاث ا هـ. وأطلق البطلان فأفاد عدم وقوع الطلاق وأن الأمر خرج من يدها لاشتغالها بما لا يعنيها.
And likewise, if he said 'unless so-and-so wills one' and so-and-so is not present, then he has that in his council of knowledge. Likewise, if he said 'you are divorced three times unless so-and-so sees otherwise', it is confined to the council. Likewise, if he said 'if so-and-so does not see otherwise', and likewise, if he said 'if so-and-so sees that', it is confined to the council. He did not mention, like most authors, what if he conditioned it on his own will, and mentioned it in al-Dhakhira: if he said 'you are divorced three times unless I see otherwise', this is not confined to the council; even if after standing from the council he says 'I saw otherwise', the three do not occur. Likewise, if he said 'unless I myself will otherwise', this is not confined to the council. And if he said to his wife 'you are divorced if so-and-so wills' or 'if he loves' or 'if he is pleased' or 'if he desires' or 'if he intends', and it reaches so-and-so, he has his council of knowledge, contrary to if he said 'if I will' or 'if I love', it is not confined to the council. The difference is that the requirement of analogy regarding a third party is that it should not be confined to the council like other conditions, but we abandoned analogy regarding the third party because it is a tamlik in meaning, and the response to tamlik is confined to the council. This meaning does not apply to the husband because the husband was the owner of divorce before this, so tamlik does not apply; thus this condition in the husband's case remains attached to other conditions, so it is not confined to the council in the husband's case. And if he said 'if I will', then how should the husband say it so that divorce occurs? Muhammad did not mention this issue in any of the books. Our mashayikh said: He should say 'I will that which I made to myself', and it is not a condition that he intend divorce when saying 'I will', nor is it a condition that he say 'I will your divorce', because divorce does not occur by his saying 'I will'; rather, it occurs by the previous statement because the divorce by the previous statement is conditional on a will considered a pure condition. So when he says 'I will', divorce occurs by the previous statement. The summary is that the husband conditioning divorce on a quality of his own heart is not a delegation or tamlik in any way. And if he said to her 'you are divorced if so-and-so does not will', and so-and-so says 'I do not will' in the council, she is divorced. And if he said that to himself, then said 'I do not will', she is not divorced. The difference is that by the third party's saying 'I do not will', despair occurs regarding the condition of fulfillment, which is the will of her divorce in the council, and it becomes substituted in terms of ruling and consideration by his saying 'I do not will' because he engaged in what he does not need for effecting divorce, because for effecting divorce, silence about the will until he stands from the council suffices. As for the husband's saying 'I do not will', despair does not occur regarding what is the condition of fulfillment because the council, although it is substituted in terms of ruling, the condition of fulfillment in the husband's case is the absence of will throughout life, and life remains. So the divorce does not fall. In al-Jami' by al-Sadr al-Shahid: He said: 'I will' or 'when' you are divorced if so-and-so wills or intends or is pleased or desires, it is confined to his council of knowledge because it is tamlik, contrary to its attachment to himself. And if he said 'if he does not will' or 'if he does not intend', and he stands from his council or says in it 'I do not will', she is divorced, contrary to 'if he does not will today'. And if he said 'if I do not will' or 'if I do not intend', and he stands or says 'I do not will', she is not divorced before his death, contrary to 'if I refuse your divorce' or 'I detest'. In al-Khaniyyah: 'You are divorced three times and so-and-so once, if you will'. So she willed one; for so-and-so, one divorce occurred, and the three are annulled for her. He mentioned annulment absolutely, indicating non-occurrence of divorce and that the matter left her hand because she engaged in what does not concern her.
Source pages p. 586

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling with proof regarding divorce conditioned on the will of a third party versus the husband's own will. The author explains that when divorce is conditioned on the will of an outsider (like 'if so-and-so wills'), the condition is confined to the council (majlis) in which the outsider learns of the condition. This is because by analogy to other conditions, it should not be confined, but the Hanafi scholars abandoned analogy in favor of istihsan, treating it as a form of tamlik (delegation). A tamlik must be responded to in the same council. However, when the husband conditions divorce on his own will (e.g., 'unless I see otherwise'), it is not confined to the council; it remains effective throughout his life. The proof for this distinction is that the husband is already the owner of divorce, so he cannot delegate it to himself. The condition remains a pure condition, like any other, not confined to any council. The passage also discusses practical details: if the husband says 'if so-and-so does not will', and the third party says 'I do not will' in the council, the divorce falls because the condition is fulfilled. But if the husband says that to himself and then says 'I do not will', it does not fall because the condition (absence of will) is for his entire life, not just the council. The text also quotes al-Jami' and al-Khaniyyah with additional examples and rulings.

Key Terms
تمليك (tamlik): Delegation or granting of the power to effect divorce to another (the wife or a third party).
مجلس العلم (majlis al-'ilm): The council or session in which the third party learns of the condition attached to the divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the ruling when a husband says 'You are divorced unless I see otherwise'?
A) The condition is confined to the council; if he doesn't see otherwise in the council, divorce falls.
B) The condition is not confined to the council; he may see otherwise at any time and the divorce will not fall.
C) The divorce falls immediately because it is a conditional statement.
D) The condition is invalid because it depends on his own will.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a third-party condition on divorce is confined to the council (as a delegation), while the husband's own condition is not confined; the distinction rests on the concept of tamlik and the application of istihsan over qiyas.
