Divorce When Condition is Already Fulfilled and the Ruling of 'Mata Shiy't'
Rules on divorce when condition is already realized and effect of 'mata shiy't'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a conditional divorce with an already fulfilled condition takes effect immediately, and explain the effect of the phrase 'mata shiy't' along with the internal Hanafi disagreement on its nature.

Core Idea
A condition already met is immediate; 'whenever you will' grants one divorce at any time, but is it tamlik or ta'liq?

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses two specific issues in conditional divorce. First, when the condition of a divorce is already fulfilled at the time of utterance, the divorce is effective immediately (tunjiz). Second, when a husband says 'you are divorced whenever you will' (mata shiy't), the wife has the power to divorce herself at any time, but only once. The passage also presents an internal Hanafi debate between al-Hidayah and Fath al-Qadir on whether this is a true tamlik or a disguised ta'liq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first ruling and its proof
Find the statement about divorce when the condition is already in existence. Note that the author says it is immediate (tunjiz) because the condition is already realized. The proof is the principle that conditioning on what exists is like unconditionality. Also note the analogy with ibra'.
2
Analyze the kufr example as a boundary case
The author contrasts divorce with declaring someone an unbeliever. Even if the condition is already true, declaring someone an unbeliever does not make them an unbeliever because unbelief requires a change in belief. However, in divorce, the ruling of the wording applies immediately.
This shows that different subject matters have different legal effects.
3
Understand the Hidayah position on 'mata shiy't'
The passage states that according to al-Hidayah, 'mata shiy't' is not confined to the council, cannot be revoked, and gives only one divorce. The reasoning is that the phrase generalizes times but not actions.
This is the relied-upon view.
4
Examine the criticism from Fath al-Qadir and the response
Fath al-Qadir argues that 'mata shiy't' is a conditional (ta'liq), not tamlik, because the husband explicitly conditions divorce on her will. The author of al-Bahr al-Raiq defends al-Hidayah by saying that scholars treated it as tamlik for convenience, as the wife's will brings about the divorce as if she owned it.
This highlights the principle of istihsan in Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإن كان لشيء مضى طلقت) يعني لو قالت المرأة شئت إن كان فلان قد جاء وقد جاء طلقت لأن التعليق بالكائن تنجيز ولذا صح تعليق الإبراء بكائن والمراد من الماضي المحقق وجوده، سواء كان ماضياً أو حاضراً كقولها شئت إن كان أبي في الدار وهو فيها أو إن كان هذا ليلاً وهي في الليل أو نهاراً وهي في النهار أو كان هذا أبي أو أمي أو زوجي وكان هو، ولا يرد أنه لو قال هو كافر إن كنت فعلت كذا وهو يعلم أن قد فعله فإنه يقتضي على هذا الكفر مع أن المختار أنه لا يكفر لأن الكفر يبتنى على تبدل الاعتقاد وتبدله غير واقع مع ذلك الفعل كما في فتح القدير، وذكر أنه الأوجه. فإن قيل : لو قال هو كافر بالله ولم يتبدل اعتقاده يجب أن يكفر فليكفر هنا بلفظ هو كافر وإن لم يتبدل اعتقاده قلنا : النازل عند وجود الشرط حكم اللفظ لا عينه فليس هو متكلماً بعد وجود الشرط بقوله هو كافر حقيقة ا هـ . والحاصل أن اللفظ الموجب للتكفير لا يحتاج إلى تبدل الاعتقاد بخلاف ما إذا كان معلقاً بالشرط ولو كان كائناً قوله : (أنت طالق متى شئت أو متى ما أو إذا أو إذا ما فردت الأمر لا يرتد ولا يتقيد بالمجلس ولا تطلق إلا (واحدة أما في كلمة متى و متى ما فلأنها للوقت وهي عامة في الأوقات كلها كأنه قال في أي وقت شئت فلا يقتصر على المجلس، ولو ردت الأمر لم يكن رداً لأنه ملكها الطلاق في الوقت الذي شاءت فلم يكن تمليكاً قبل المشيئة حتى يرتد بالرد، ولا تطلق نفسها إلا واحدة لأنها تعم الأزمان دون الأفعال فتملك التطليق في كل زمان، ولا تملك تطليقاً بعد تطليق. كذا في الهداية. وتعقبه في فتح القدير بأن هذا ليس تمليكاً في حال أصلاً لأنه بطلاقها معلقاً صرح بشرط مشيئتها ، فإذا وجدت مشيئتها وقع طلاقه. وإنما يصح ما ذكره في طلقي نفسك متى شئت لأنها تتصرف بحكم الملك بخلاف ما لو قالت طلقت نفسي في هذه المسألة فإنه وإن وقع الطلاق لكن الواقع طلاقه المعلق، وقولها طلقت إيجاد للشرط الذي هو مشيئة الطلاق على تقدير أن المشيئة تقارن الإيجاد ا هـ . وجوابه أن هذا وإن كان تعليقاً لكن أجروه مجرى التمليك في جميع الوجوه فيتقيد بالمجلس ويبطل بما يدل على الاعراض قال المقدسي : لا يخفى أن محصل الجواب أنهم تسامحوا وجعلوا تعليق الطلاق بمشيئتها ونحوها في حكم التمليك لكونها إذا شاءت وقع فكأنها ملكته، وهذا
His saying: (And if it was for something past, she is divorced) means if the woman said 'I will, if so-and-so has come', and he had come, then she is divorced, because conditioning on what is already in existence is immediate (tunjiz). For this reason, conditioning of absolution (ibra') on something existent is valid. What is meant by the past is what is already realized, whether it is past or present, like her saying 'I will, if my father is in the house' and he is in it, or 'if this is night' and it is night, or 'if this is day' and it is day, or 'if this is my father, mother, or husband' and it is him. And it is not countered that if he said 'he is an unbeliever if you did such and such' while he knows that he did it, it would necessitate unbelief on this basis, while the chosen view is that he does not become an unbeliever because unbelief is based on change of belief, and change is not realized with that action as mentioned in Fath al-Qadir, and he mentioned that it is the most appropriate. If it is said: If he said 'he is an unbeliever by Allah' and his belief did not change, he should become an unbeliever, so let him become an unbeliever here by the wording 'he is an unbeliever' even if his belief did not change? We say: What descends upon the existence of the condition is the ruling of the wording, not its essence. So he is not actually a speaker of 'he is an unbeliever' after the condition exists. The summary is that the wording causing unbelief does not require a change of belief, unlike if it is conditional on a condition even if it is already existing. His saying: (You are divorced whenever you will) or 'whenever' or 'if' or 'when'. If she refuses the command, it is not a refusal, and it is not confined to the council, and she is not divorced except one. For the word 'whenever' and 'whenever' is for time, and it is general for all times, as if he said 'at any time you will', so it is not confined to the council. And if she refused the command, it is not a refusal because he made her the owner of divorce at the time she wills, so it was not a tamlik before the will such that it can be revoked by refusal. And she cannot divorce herself except one because it generalizes times, not actions. So she owns the divorce in every time, but she does not own a divorce after a divorce. This is in al-Hidayah. And it was commented upon in Fath al-Qadir that this is not a tamlik at all, because he explicitly conditioned divorce on her will. So when her will occurs, his divorce falls. What he mentioned is only valid in 'divorce yourself whenever you will' because she acts by virtue of ownership, contrary to saying 'I divorced myself' in this issue, for even if the divorce falls, what falls is his conditional divorce, and her saying 'I divorced' is effecting the condition which is the will of divorce on the assumption that the will accompanies the effecting. The answer is that although this is a conditional (ta'liq), they treated it as tamlik in all respects, so it is confined to the council and is invalidated by anything indicating rejection. Al-Maqdisi said: It is not hidden that the gist of the answer is that they were lenient and made the conditional divorce on her will and the like in the ruling of tamlik, because when she wills, it falls, so it is as if she owned it. And this...
Source pages p. 587

Commentary
This passage contains two main rulings. First, if the condition of the divorce is already fulfilled at the time of utterance, the divorce takes effect immediately (tunjiz), because a condition on an already existing fact is treated as an unconditional statement. The author gives examples: if the wife says 'I will, if my father is in the house' and he is in the house, the divorce falls. This is contrasted with the case of declaring someone an unbeliever based on a condition that is already true; there, the correct view is that unbelief does not occur because it is based on actual belief, not just a statement. However, in divorce, the ruling of the wording applies. Second, the ruling on the phrase 'you are divorced whenever you will' (mata shiy't). According to al-Hidayah, this is not confined to the council, cannot be revoked by the wife's refusal, and she can only divorce herself once, because the phrase generalizes times but not actions. Fath al-Qadir criticizes this view, arguing that it is not a tamlik but a conditional, so the wife's will simply fulfills the condition. The author of al-Bahr al-Raiq defends the Hidayah view by noting that scholars treated it as tamlik for convenience, because the wife's will brings about the divorce as if she owned it. The passage ends with al-Maqdisi's summary that this is a lenient treatment.

Key Terms
متى شئت (mata shiy't): A phrase meaning 'whenever you will', used to give the wife the power to divorce herself at any time she chooses.
تنجيز (tunjiz): Immediate effect; a conditional statement where the condition is already fulfilled is treated as unconditional and takes effect immediately.
تعليق (ta'liq): Conditional; a statement whose effect is suspended upon the occurrence of a condition.
تمليك (tamlik): Delegation; granting ownership or authority to another, in this context giving the wife the power to divorce herself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a husband says 'You are divorced whenever you will' (متى شئت), how many divorces can the wife effect?
A) One divorce only.
B) Three divorces, one at a time.
C) Unlimited as long as she wills.
D) It depends on the husband's intent.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a divorce conditioned on an already existing fact is immediate, and the phrase 'mata shiy't' is treated as a delegation of one divorce at any time, with the prevailing view that it is akin to tamlik.

Conditional Divorce on Another's Will: Delegation and Its Implications
Ruling on conditional divorce linked to another's will with variations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that a conditional divorce attached to another's will is treated as tamlik (delegation) restricted to the gathering (majlis) and apply the different outcomes for variations in phrasing and the multiple-wife scenario.

Core Idea
Divorce conditional on another's will is delegation, limited to the gathering; phrasings change the outcome.

When a husband conditions divorce on the will of a third person, the Hanafi school classifies this as tamlik (delegation) rather than pure ta'liq. This crucial distinction restricts the divorce to the gathering (majlis) where the statement is made. The passage then explores variations—negative conditions, time limits, and cases involving multiple wives—each with distinct rulings based on the same underlying principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition and its legal characterization
The husband's statement 'if so-and-so wills' is legally treated as tamlik (delegation) because the power to divorce is given to that person's will.
The illa is that the will of another is effectively being empowered.
2
Apply the rule of tamlik: restriction to the majlis
Since tamlik is a delegation, it is confined to the gathering (majlis) where the delegation occurred. If the person remains silent or the gathering ends, no divorce occurs.
Contrast with ta'liq to self, which is unrestricted.
3
Analyze negative conditions on another's will
If the husband says 'if he does not will' and the person stands or says 'I do not wish' in the gathering, the divorce occurs because the condition (negation) is fulfilled.
Exception: 'if he does not wish today' delays until end of day.
4
Examine conditions on the husband's own will
If the husband says 'if I do not will' and he stands or says 'I do not wish', the divorce does not occur until his death.
Contrast with direct refusal 'I refuse your divorce' which triggers immediate divorce.
5
Handle the multiple-wife scenario from al-Khaniyah
If a man says to one wife 'you are thrice divorced' and to another 'once divorced if she wills', and the second wills one, only she is divorced once; the three are annulled because she busied herself with what does not concern her.
The principle: exceeding the scope of delegation voids the entire conditional statement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق فصل في المشيئة شئت أو متى أنت طالق إن شاء فلان أو أراد أو رضي أو هوى فيقتصر على مجلس علمه لأنه تمليك بخلاف إضافته إلى نفسه ولو قال إن لم يشأ أو إن لم يرد فقام من مجلسه أو قال فيه لا أشاء طلقت بخلاف إن لم يشأ اليوم، ولو قال إن لم أشأ إن لم أرد فقام أو قال لا أشاء لا تطلق قبل موته بخلاف إن أبيت طلاقك أو كرهت ا هـ . وفي الخانية : أنت طالق ثلاثاً وفلانة واحدة إن شئت فشاءت واحدة لفلانة طلقت فلانة واحدة ويبطل عنها الثلاث ا هـ. وأطلق البطلان فأفاد عدم وقوع الطلاق وأن الأمر خرج من يدها لاشتغالها بما لا يعنيها
Book of Divorce, Section on Conditional Will: 'If you wish' or 'whenever you are divorced if so-and-so wills, or wants, or is pleased, or desires' — it is restricted to the gathering of his knowledge because it is a delegation, unlike adding it to himself. And if he said 'if he does not will' or 'if he does not want' and he stood from his gathering or said in it 'I do not wish' she is divorced, unlike if he said 'if he does not wish today'. And if he said 'if I do not will' or 'if I do not want' and he stood or said 'I do not wish' she is not divorced before his death, unlike if he said 'I refuse your divorce or I dislike it'. And in al-Khaniyah: You are thrice divorced and so-and-so once if she wills, so if she wills one for so-and-so, so-and-so is divorced once and the three are annulled for her. And he made the annulment absolute so it indicates non-occurrence of divorce and that the matter left her hand because she busied herself with what does not concern her.
Source pages p. 587

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on conditional divorce when the condition is attached to the will of another person. The key principle is that such a statement is treated as a delegation (tamlik) to that person, and therefore it is limited to the gathering (majlis) in which the statement was made. If the husband says 'you are divorced if so-and-so wills', then only if that person expresses his will in that same gathering does the divorce take effect. This is unlike when the husband adds the condition to his own will, which is unrestricted. If the husband says 'if he does not will' or 'if he does not want', and the person stands up or says 'I do not wish' in the gathering, the divorce occurs because the condition is fulfilled. However, if he says 'if he does not wish today', the divorce is delayed until the end of the day. If the husband says 'if I do not will' or 'if I do not want', and he stands or says 'I do not wish', the divorce does not occur until his death, because his refusal is not effective as he is the one making the condition. But if he says 'I refuse your divorce' or 'I dislike it', the divorce occurs immediately. The text then cites al-Khaniyah with a case: if a man says to his wife 'you are thrice divorced, and so-and-so (another wife) is once divorced if she wills', and the other wife wills one divorce, only she is divorced once, and the three divorces for the first wife are annulled. This is because the wife acted beyond her scope, so the matter is voided.

Key Terms
تمليك (tamlik): Delegation of the power to divorce to another person, making the divorce effective upon that person's will.
مجلس علمه (majlis 'ilmihi): The gathering in which the statement of conditional divorce is made.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if a husband says to his wife: 'You are divorced if so-and-so wills'?
A) The divorce occurs immediately regardless of so-and-so's will.
B) It is treated as a delegation and limited to the gathering; if so-and-so wills in that gathering, she is divorced.
C) The wife is divorced three times automatically.
D) The divorce is invalid because it is conditional on another's will.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a conditional divorce on another's will is treated as delegation (tamlik) and limited to the gathering; different phrasings and scenarios produce distinct rulings, all based on this core principle.
